DEFINITIONS
Acceptors                      =  Persons accepting the practice of contra-
ception
Average Family Size            =  Average number of persons in the family
i.e., surviving children and parents.
Age-Specific Fertility Rates  =  Number of live births per year to 1,000
women in five-year age group during
15-49 years.
Crude Birth Rate               =  Number of live births per year per 1,000
of population.
Crude Death Rate               =  Number of deaths per 1,000 of population.
Dependency Ratio               =  Number of persons of 14 years or under,
plus 65 or over, divided by the population
aged 15 to 64 years. A frequently used
but not very satisfactory measure of the
balance between non-producing and producing
members of society.
Expectation of Life at Birth  =  Average number of years an infant, exposed
to a given set of mortality rates, is
expected to live.
General Fertility Rate         =  Number of live births per year per 1,000
women aged 15-49 years.
Gross Reproduction Rate        =  The average number of daughters a woman will
bear if she experiences a given set of
age-specific birth rates throughout the
reproductive ages, with no allowance for
mortality over this period.
Infant Mortality Rate          =  Annual deaths of infants 0-12 months per
1,000 live births during the same year.
Maternal Mortality Rate         =  Number of deaths as a result of complications
of pregnancy, childbirth and the puer-
perium, per 1,000 live births.
Net Reproduction Rate          =  Same as the gross reproduction rate but
adjusted for mortality of women over their
reproductive years. A net reproduction
rate of 1.0 when reached, indicates that
the country will be able to reach zero
population growth rate after five to six
decades. The population continues to in-
crease after NRR has reached 1, because of
the "above-replacement" daughters already
born before the NRR fell to 1.0.
Rate of Population Growth      =  Rate of natural increase adjusted for (net)
immigration or emigration.
Rate of Natural Increase        m  Difference between crude birth and crude
death rate - usually expressed as a per-
centage.
Total Fertility Rate           =  The average number of children a woman will
have if she experiences a given set of
age-specific birth rates throughout her
reproductive life. A good index to measure
fertility changes as it is independent of
age and sex distribution.

The report is based on the findings of the two missions that visited
Bangladesh in May-June 1973 and November 1973. The May-June mission con-
sisted of Mr. G. B. Baldwin (Chief), Dr. T. I. Kim (Deputy Chief), Miss I.Z.
Husain (rapporteur), Messrs J. Ratcliffe (Bank), I.H. Kang (Bank), B.A. Jenny
(Consultant-Management), G. Louvell (Consultant-Architect), Dr. Raja Rao
(Consultant-UNFPA) and Mr. C.H. Weeks (Consultant-Communication). Dr. K.
Kanagaratnam joined the mission during the last week. Dr. Sam Street,
Country Representative for WHO, participated in the field and in subsequent
discussions in Washington.  The November mission consisted of Miss I.Z. Husain
(Bank), J.E. Lundeberg (Bank) and R.P. Worrall (Consultant-Communication).
The yellow cover draft of the appraisal report was discussed with the Govern-
ment during April 14 to May 1, 1974 by a mission that consisted of Mr. G. B.
Baldwin, Miss I.Z. Husain and Mr. J. E. Lundeberg. Subsequently Mr. Alex
Shaw (Communication Specialist - Bank) visited Bangladesh from July 2-9, 1974
to discuss the details of some components of the project.

APPRAISAL REPORT OF A POPULATION PROJECT IN BANGLADESH
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The report appraises a population project for Bangladesh for which
an IDA Credit of US$15 million equivalent has been proposed. The total
project cost is estimated at US$45.7 million. The main objective of the
project is to assist the Government in translating the broad-based population
policy defined in the First Five-Year Plan (1973-78) into a national pro-
gram. At present the country has a minimal population planning program that
is barely functioning. The Government's first task under the Five-Year
Population Plan is to work out a feasible program, to organize itself for
carrying out this program, to secure financing, and to get the program
moving.
ii.       The implications of a natural population growth rate of 3% per
annum in a country with a thin resource base whose people have a per capita
income of only about US$70 and over one-fourth of whose labor force is chronically
unemployed are serious. Recognizing these problems, the Government in its
First Five-Year Plan (1973-78) stated that no 'civilized measure would be
too drastic to keep the population of Bangladesh on the smaller side of 15
crores (150 million) for sheer ecological viability of the nation.'
iii.      The proposed project is designed to assist the Government (a)
in establishing a health delivery system to meet the existing demand for
family planning services and (b) in developing a comprehensive national popula-
tion program addressed equally to the creation of demand for family planning
and to the delivery of services. For the former, it will provide and train
the health and family planning field workers. For the latter it will (a)
expand the capacity for training health and family planning manpower needed
for building a nation-wide service delivery program, (b) establish a number
of pilot schemes for introducing family life education and motivation activ-
ities in the development programs of five ministries, (c) strengthen the
capacity of the mass media to deal with population topics, and (d) build up
research and evaluation activities to provide the knowledge needed for future
program expansion.
iv.       The project consists of:
(a)  the construction, furnishing and equipment of (i) one
college of nursing for nurse tutor training (ii) eight
schools for training family welfare visitors (a category
of paramedical personnel in Bangladesh), (iii) eight
new thana health complexes with clinical facilities and
a 25-bed maternity ward unit for field training of medical
and paramedical personnel, (iv) 24 union health subcenters
for field training and (v) four model family planning
clinics in medical colleges for the training of medical
students;



(b) training and salary support for 3,700 female family
welfare workers who will deliver maternal and child
health and family planning services through the
home visiting program in rural areas;
(c) the establishment and support of nine education/motivation
pilot schemes in 61 thanas (counties) to integrate
population programs in the development activities of
the five different ministries such as training of
model farmers and cooperative managers, rural women's
functional literacy program, mothers' clubs, agricultural
assistants field visits; labor welfare centers, female
family welfare workers' home visits, a publicity program
through audiovisual vans; a mobile population education
team and a women's vocational training program;
(d) strengthening the mass media activities of the Ministry
of Information and Broadcasting by establishing a
population program cell in Radio Bangladesh, expanding
the capacity of the Department of Films, setting up
a Population Feature Writing Bureau for the Press Infor-
mation Department; and training of public relations
officers in population problem;
(e) initiating curriculum development and teachers' training
for the introduction of population education in the
primary and secondary schools;
(f) conducting seminars on population education for schools
and on population problems for political leaders
and other government officials;
(g) strengthening the institutions responsible for the
evaluation of the program through the establishment
of an Evaluation Unit at the Health and Population
Planning Section (PPS) of the Planning Commission,
supporting the evaluation units of the ministries
responsible for different schemes and establishing
a system of analysis of vital statistics and service
statistics collected by the Ministry of Health and
Population Planning (MOHPP);
(h)  The provision of a management study at the MOHPP; and the
appointment of the population program officers to be
responsible for the pilot schemes in the various ministries,
their study tour abroad and advisory services for them, and
a building utilization study;



- Lit -
Wi) a study on the determinants of reproductive behavior
through the Bangladesh Institute of Development Studies
(BIDS) in association with the universities, and a study of
the national laws for their pronatality impact; and
(j) support to the private sector and for innovative activities
in the public sector (in an unallocated amount) to be
identified during the life of the project.
The total project cost of US$45.7 million equivalent, with a foreign
e^rhdnge component of 53X, will be financed by an IDA credit of US$15 million
by grants of about US$25.4 million equivalent from six bilateral donors,
ano by a Government contribution of US$5.3 million. All donors would finance
spectfic components of the project (parallel financing), except the Government
cl Australia (joint financing with IDA) and the Swedish International Develop-
ment Authority (SIDA) (joint financing with the Government of Bangladesh).
vt,       IDA disbursements will be based on a percentage of the actual costs
of specified project components. IDA will also disburse the funds of the
donors (except SIDA) who have elected IDA to serve as their disbursing agent
for this project. For this purpose. the establishment of a Trust Fund bv
the concerned donors is being proposed from which IDA would be authorized to
draw. To speed up procurement, the Government will establish a revolving
fund in Takas for payment of import duties, local taxes, cost of consultants
and advisers and contingencies.
vfi.      Overall responsibility for project administration will be borne
by the Population Planning Division (PPD) recently established at the MOHPP
in collaboration with the Health and Population Planning Section (PPS) of
the Planning Commission. The PPD will be responsible for coordinating and
monitoring the execution of project activities. The PPS will be the main
poinc of contact between IDA and the Government on all project matters and
will be responsible for the allocation of funds to internal agencies involved
In the project and for the external evaluation of project activities. To
execute project activities, all participating ministries will establish new
units or posts. The Ministry of Health and Population Planning has created
a Building Planning and Design Unit to be responsible for the construction
of all projects facilities.   Five other participating ministries will create
the new post of population program officer to carry out their newly-defined
population activities.
v4iii     Contracts for furniture, equipment, vehicles and parts, and im-
ported building material financed by IDA and the Government of Australia
will be awarded on the basis of international competitive bidding in
accznrdance with IDA procurement guidelines. In the evaluation of bids,
domciitic manufacturers will be awarded a preferential margin of 15% or the
existing rate of import duties, whichever is lower. Contractors for civil



i iv -
works will be prequalified and domestic contractors will be accorded a
preference of 7.5%.
ix.       The project's main contribution will be to help the Government
organize and start its population program. It is difficult with data
available at this time to make any meaningful estimates of the project's
probable demographic impact. However, some crude estimates suggest that the
program's demographic objectives with best possible utilization of existing
resources and continuation of activities generated in the project would avert
only about 23 million births by the end of the century - one-third of the
number required to be averted by that time to achieve the desired reduction
in fertility. The project itself will be responsible for averting directly
only about 1.3 million births. This does not include the indirect contribution
of the project by generating certain long-term program activities. In
addition, the project may result in a significant improvement in overall
maternal and child health care that will contribute indirectly to fertility
regulation, and this is in itself desirable.



APPRAISAL REPORT OF A POPULATION PROJECT - BANGLADESH
I. INTRODUCTION
A. Background
1.01      The project covered by this appraisal report is the first Bank
Group assistance to the population sector in Bangladesh. A separate sector
report contains more detailed background information on the demographic
situation of the country and on the Government's Five-Year Population Plan,
(1973-78).
B. The Population Problem
1.02      The concern for slowing down population growth is based on the
presently bleak, if not desperate, long-run economic and social outlook for
the country. Historically, the precarious balance between population and
resources has been maintained by depressingly large numbers of premature
deaths due to disease, famine and war. The situation is likely to grow
worse fairly rapidly if the present current high rate of population growth
continues. The Planning Commission has estimated a total population of
74 million as of January 1973 and a natural rate of increase of 3% per
annum, resulting from a crude birth rate of 47 per thousand and a death
rate of 17 per thousand. 1/ There is a small net emigration, so that the
growth of population is estimated at about 2.9%.
1.03      About 90% of the population is rural with 80% engaged in agriculture;
these proportions have hardly changed during the last two decades. Per capita
income is estimated at about US$72 per year, among the lowest in the world.
The literacy rate is about 23% of the total population. Unemployment is high
and persistent at over one-fourth of the total labor force. The country's
main natural resource is agricultural land, of which the average farm family
has only 0.45 acres for each of its members, an extremely low ratio. With
national population threatening to more than double, reaching in excess of
160 million within the short span of 23 years, and with no significant amount
of additional land available for cultivation, the prospects for a peasantry
already at the margin of subsistence are grim. (Annex 1) The country is
presently heavily dependent upon imported food supplies. A recent Bank
Economic Report (September, 1974) suggests the attainment of some form of
food grain self-sufficiency within 10-12 years. However, it adds that, in
1/   The February 1974 census, which is being checked for underenumeration,
reported a population of 71.3 million. The 1961 census (without adjust-
ment for underenumeration) reported a population of 50.8 million.
These two figures yield an average exponential population growth rate
of 2.6% per annum. The Planning Commission estimated a population of
76 million for 1974.



-2-
the long run, the gap between demand and supply will emerge once again even
on the optimistic assumption of an increase in agricultural output and a
decline in population growth rates. Thus, the country would be expected
to once again become dependent on imports by the end of the century. A sub-
sistence economy permits little more than a subsistence government budget,
with a huge chronic gap between needs and resources and the prospect of con-
tinuing heavy dependence on external aid even after the present reconstruction
period is over. A high population growth rate will, of course, only widen
the gap between needs and resources.
II. GOVERNMENT POLICIES AND PROGRAMS
A. Background
2.01      The country's family planning program began with voluntary efforts
in the early 1950s. A nation-wide government program that started in 1960
under the Ministry of Health as a part of the health services made little
headway during its first five years. In 1965 a reorganization took the
family planning program out of the Ministry of Health and established it as
an independent, single-purpose program with its own structure. There was
an East Pakistan Provincial Family Planning Board, in addition to Family
Planning Boards at the district and thana (county) levels. While doctors
played an important part, particularly in sterilization, the program was
one of the few in the world to be organized outside the Ministry of Health.
The Provincial Family Planning Board was made responsible for the national
program with three other supporting organizations that had overall responsi-
bility for the functions indicated in their titles.  These were the Directo-
rate of Training, Research, Evaluation and Communication (called TREC), the
Inspection Directorate (responsible for supervision) and the Postpartum
Directorate. The lady family planning visitors (LFPVs), largely trained
for IUD insertions, were used widely in the program.
2.02      Although the program in the late 1960s achieved some considerable
statistical success in recruiting acceptors, there is at present widespread
dissatisfaction with many features of the earlier program. The main feature
of the earlier program (now discredited) was its extensive reliance on
substantial incentive payments to both the personnel involved in administer-
ing the program and to acceptors. Despite these reported shortcomings, there
is one extremely important legacy left by the earlier program: a large
proportion of the population became aware of what family planning is and what
the possibilities are for limiting fertility. This knowledge seems quite
pervasive, even in the rural areas, and provides an invaluable base on which
to build for the future. Pakistan's Fourth Five-Year Plan (1970-75) was to



-3-
bring certain changes to the program, but implementation was delayed until
after the elections of 1970. Then came the Civil War which resulted in
Bangladesh achieving its independence in December 1971. The war severely
disrupted almDst all aspects of national life, including the family planning
program. (Annex 2)
B. Existing Situation
2.03      The main feature in the population sector since Independence has
been the search for new organizational and administrative structures at
national, district and thana levels. However, following Independence the
Family Planning Board (renamed the National Family Planning Board - NFPB),
along with TREC and the Postpartum and Inspection Directorates, were placed
under the Ministry of Health, which was renamed the Ministry of Health and
Family Planning (MOHFP). An interministerial board (also called the NFPB) was
formed in 1972 for policy-making, program guidance and coordination of
activities with other ministries (Chart 8452). So far, however, the Board
has not met and consequently has given no direction to the program. There
were about 400 separate family planning clinics under the NFPB offering some
kind of family planning services, but the level of activity has been drastic-
ally reduced since the war. This stalemate results from the fact that while
the population policy has changed completely under the First Five-Year Plan,
the institutional set up has remained essentially the same. Recently the
Government has made some decisions on the organizational proposals which
were suggested in the Plan.
C. First Five-Year Plan Proposals
2.04      One of the demographic objectives of the Plan is to reluce the
natural rate of population growth from 3% at present to 2.8% by 1978. It is
envisaged that at the end of the Plan period the crude birth rate will decline
to 43 from the present level of 47 per thousand population with a decline in
the death rate from 17 to 15 per thousand population. The long-run goal is
to reduce the net reproduction rate to 1 in the next 25-30 years in order to
achieve zero populatiOn growth in the next century.
2.05      To achieve the above objectives the Plan suggests that:
(a) The family planning program, largely in the sense of delivery
of services, should be integrated with health services and
the MOHFP should be responsible for it. The MOHFP should
undertake the delivery of basic health and family planning
services through a home visiting scheme in rural areas



- 4 -
services through a home visiting scheme in rural areas
employing about 12,000 teams of workers (male and female)
the MOHFP should plan to establish one rural health
center and three subcenters in all 356 rural thanas
during the Five-Year Plan period for the delivery of
health and family planning services. (At present there
are about 160 rural health centers and 186 subcenters.)
(b) A population planning educational and motivational
effort should be made part of the program of all
ministries that maintain wide public contact, such as
the Ministries of Local Government, Rural Development,
and Cooperatives,Agriculture, Education, Labor and
Social Welfare, and Information and Broadcasting.
(c) All voluntary organizations involved in family planning should
be given adequate encouragement and support.
(d) The Government should consider a strong disincentive after the
second child, legalizing abortion and making it free of cost,
and should undertake social measures for the emancipation of
women.
2.06      For the implementation of the above policies the Plan proposed that
the MOHFP should play an important role in the delivery of services and that
other relevant ministries should develop the capability to contribute to
population planning education and motivation. This multi-sectoral population
program, should be coordinated by a central unit to be named the Population
Planning Division. The Plan is silent on the location of this division.
In the Plan it is suggested that inter-ministerial coordination committees
at all levels of program implementation - national, district and thana -
be formed. Furthermore, the population policy, should be formulated by a
high-level National Population Council (NPC) and consist of ministers of
relevant ministries and other public leaders and chaired by the Prime Minister.
(Annex 2 - Chart 8951R).
2.07      Recently the Government has decided to establish the Population
Planning Division (PPD) proposed above in the Ministry of Health and Family
Planning (now renamed the Ministry of Health and Population - MOHPP). The
PPD has been established as a separate division under the MOHPP with a
full-time secretary as its head. Thus the MOHPP will now have two divisions:
(a) the Health Division (lID), responsible for delivery of integrated healtl
and family planning services and (b) the PPD, responsible for coordinating
and monitoring the population programs of different ministries and agencies
as well as providing technical support to these agencies in programming and
evaluating their population-related activities. It is reported that the
staff and resources of the existing TREC, Inspection Directorate and some from



-5-
the NFPB will be transferred from the Health Division to the PPD. The
expected organizational structure of the MOHPP is given in Annex 2,
Chart 9486.
2s08      An attempt will be made to develop population activities in five
ministries other than the MOHPP. Population program officers and supporting
staff will be appointed in each ministry as part of the project. In addition
to the PPD, the existing Health and Family Planning Division of the Planning
Covmission (now renamed the Health and Population Planning Section - PPS) will
be responsible for external evaluation of all program activities, allocation
of funds to the concerned ministries, determination of the intra-sectoral
priorities and allocation of funds to different agencies based on progress
of the activities and liason with external donors. Among the committees
mentioned in para 2.06, only the Central Coordination Committee at the national
level has been formed by a recent order of the Government (Annex 2). No
decision has yet been taken on the formation of the NPC or other committees.
D. External Assistance
2.09      The Planning Commission has allocated TK 700 million (US$93
million) for the population program during the First Five-Year Plan (1973-
78). This expenditure target is to be financed from the national budget
and from several foreign donors. The UNFPA project, for which an agreement
has been signed for three years, includes US$5.2 million for eight components
that have been worked out in considerable detail, plus US$4.8 million for
two broad components for which details are to be worked out later. So far
the largest bilateral assistance was from the United States Agency for Inter-
national Development (USAID), which in 1972 and 1973 made US$6.5 million
available for health facilities, manpower retraining, medical equipment,
and contraceptive supplies. USAID is likely to make another US$8-10 million
directly available to the program over the 1974-77 period. Several private
agencies will also make important financial and technical assistance con-
tributions to the program; these include the Ford Foundation in the field
of research (US$300,000 for two years) and the Pathfinder Fund in postpartum
motivation (US$1.5 million). (Annex 3)  There does not seem to be any over-
lap among donor activities and the present project. In fact, these activities
appear to be complementary.
III.  THE PROJECT
A. Objectives and Strategy
3.01      The project is designed to assist the development of a comprehensive
national fertility-control program. It will expand facilities and staff to
serve the existing demand for family planning services and will launch a wide



-6-
set of activities designed to create additional demand for such services.
The following considerations underlie project development:
(a) A major constraint on the expansion of health and family
planning services is the shortage of intermediate level
health manpower i.e., nurses and family welfare visitors
(FWV - a category of paramedical manpower utilized for health
and family planning). Bangladesh is unique in its adverse
ratio of doctors to nurses, estimated at 8:1. The shortage
of such manpower is mainly the result of a limited physical
capacity for training tutors, nurses and FWVs. The
Government has established 13 new schools for the training
of nurses; but the increased number of nurses is likely
to be absorbed by the hospitals in urban areas. Therefore,
the main reliance in the rural areas will have to be on FWVs
for a long time. The project supplements this national
effort by the construction of one college of nursing for
the training of nurse tutors (who will also teach FWVs) and
eight schools for the basic training of FWVs, who will be
the key staff in the rural health scheme. In addition,
eight thana health complexes with 24 subcenters will provide
field training for FWVs and medical students and four model
family planning clinics in medical colleges will give
practical training to medical students in family planning
methods.
(b) The home visiting program of the team of field workers is the
main source at present for the distribution of pills, condoms
and other methods as well as for other simple health services.
The project provides training and salary support for the
female members of the team who would be mainly responsible
for maternal and child health and family planning services.
(c) The Government's policy of changing social attitudes towards
family size by integrating the population planning program with
the development programs of individual ministries is based on
evidence that family planning programs which are integrated
with other development efforts have a better chance of success.
The project will help the Government integrate family planning
motivation and education with schemes designed to (i) raise
the economic and social status of women and (ii) train village
leaders in rural development programs. The total number of
thanas covered during the project period by different schemes
would be about 61, or 15% of the total 413 thanas. These
thanas have been selected to cover the major administrative,
geographic and socio-economic conditions found in the country.
The schemes dealing with raising the socio-economic status



-7-
- 7 -
of women are new and would be initiated in only a limited
number of villages in selected thanas. The project supports
these schemes because of their possible direct bearing on
reproductive behavior. Schemes dealing with training village
leaders are already in operation; the project will add a
family planning training element in ongoing training programs
for all the villages of the selected thanas.
(d) Films and radios are found to be popular mass media in rural
areas. The project creates the capacity for the production of
family planning films and radio broadcasts - the contents of
which will be guided by the results of action-oriented attitude
research.
(e) Introduction of population education in the primary and
secondary school systems is necessary to create awareness of
population problems among future parents. The project
supports the development of curriculum and teacher-training
for this purpose.
(f) At this stage of program development a strong system of evalua-
tion of all program components is crucial. The project would
support an internal evaluation (by the implementing ministries
and the PPD) as well as an external evaluation by the Health
and Population Planning Section of the Planning Commission.
(g) For the long-term development of the population program, a
study on the determinants of reproductive behavior to assess
the factors influencing the family-size decision is essential.
The project supports these studies. In addition the project
also supports a study of existing laws for their pronatality
impact.
(h) The success of both the program and the project will depend
on viable organizational structures for their implementation,
coupled with a strong political commitment. The project incliides
a management study for the review of the present organization
of the MOHPP.  For implementation of the project in minist-- es
other than the MOHPP, the project will establish the position
of population program officer. The overall coordination,
evaluation and monitoring of project activities will be the
responsibility of the PPD in the MOHPP in collaboration with
the PPS in the Planning Commission.      A seminar on popu-
lation problems for political leaders is included in the
project for creating awareness of the gravity of population
problems. The project also includes various studies and
technical assistance to help reduce construction time and
costs.



B. Summary of Prolect Composition
3.02      The project will consist of the following components:
(a)  Technical assistance and general support for strengthening
the population program organization.
(i)  Obtaining the services of a management firm to
assist the Ministry of Health and Population
Planning in improving its internal organization;
(ii) Conducting a national seminar on population problems
for political leaders; and
(iii) Conducting studies on building utilization.
(b) Constructing, furnishing, equipping and supporting the following
training and service-cum-training facilities for the Ministry
of Health and Population Planning.
(i)  One college of nursing in Dacca to graduate 60 tutors
annually to train nurses and public health nursing staff;
(ii) Eight family welfare visitor training schools to train 60
students annually;
(iii) Eight new thana health complexes with (a) a 25-bed maternity
ward unit, (b) dormitories for 25 trainees, (c) an outpatient
clinic and (d) staff housing to serve as rural field training
centers for medical and paramedical personnel in health
and family planning services;
(iv)  Twenty-four union health subcenters with dormitory capacity
for two trainees for union-level field training (three per
health complex described above); and
(v)  Four model clinics in medical colleges for family planning
training of medical students, and the retraining of 50
medical students annually.
(c)  Training and subsequent employment for four years for
3,700 female family welfare workers as members of male/
female home-visiting teams in rural areas.
(d)  Provision of audiovisual equipment, vehicles and operating
costs for three years for the establishment and support of
nine education/motivation pilot schemes in 61 thanas and general
program support to the designated ministries.



- 9 -
Ministry of Local Government, Rural Development and Cooperatives:
(i)  A pilot scheme for the use of model farmers and cooperative
managers as change agents for family planning; and
(ii) A pilot scheme for the rural women's functional literacy
program as a means of acceptance of family planning.
Ministry of Labor and Social Welfare:
(i)  A pilot scheme for the formation of rural mothers' clubs
for family planning motivation;
(ii) A pilot scheme for the women's vocational training programs
as a means of acceptance of family planning; and
(iii) A pilot scheme for the family planning program for
organized labor.
Ministry of Aqriculture:
(i) A pilot scheme for the use of agricultural assistants
for population education.
Ministry of Education:
(i)  A pilot scheme for the use of a mobile population
education team to hold seminars on population problems
for school teachers to obtain their support in in-
fluencing the community;
(ii)  Curriculum development and teachers' training for the
introduction of population education in the primary and
secondary school curricula; and
(iii) A national seminar on population education.
Ministry of Information and Broadcasting:
(i)  A  pilot scheme for the use of audiovisual vans for
family planning films;
(ii)  Establishing a Population Program Cell for Radio Bangladesh;
(iii) Expansion of the film producing capacity of the Department
of Films for producing family planning films;
(iv)  Setting up a Population Feature Writing Bureau; and
(v)  Training the public relations officer in population
problems.



- 10 -
Ministry of Health and Population Planning:
(i) The pilot scheme for the use of family planning motivation
kits by family welfare workers.
(e) Salary support, operating costs, support for institutions
capable of conducting research studies and evaluation.
(i)  The establishment of an Evaluation Unit at the
Health and Population Planning Section of the Planning
Commission for program evaluation;
(ii) Special evaluation studies of the pilot schemes to be
conducted by the PPD through the concerned ministries;
(iii) A study on the determinants of reproductive
behavior by the Bangladesh Institute of Development
Studies; and
(iv) A study of national laws to assess their pronatality
impact to be conducted by PPD through a special committee.
(f) Support to the Population Planning Division to assist the private
sector in family planning activities and for the development of
innovative activities in the public sector during the life of
the Project.
C. Project Description
Program Organization and Administration
3.03      The Population Planning Division of the MOHPP has been given primary
responsibility for planning, coordinating and supervising the country's
population program. However, it will rely on the Ministry's Health Division
to provide family planning services. While the large Health Division has
been in existence for some time, the PPD has only recently been established
and at present has a limited staff. The organizational structure and the
availability of personnel at the PPD are still under consideration. Avail-
ability of a detailed organizational structure of the PPD acceptable to IDA
and provision for full-time staff and facilities are conditions of credit
effectiveness. The Health Division of the MOHPP is responsible for the
delivery of integrated health and family planning services. There are
unresolved doubts as to the suitability of its structure for efficient delivery
of these services. There are questions with regard to the organization and
personnel in the field, coordination among them, and the line of command
from headquarters. With the establishment of the PPD there is a need to



define the relationship between the two divisions themselves both at the
central and field levels and with other ministries and agencies involved in
the program. A comprehensive management study of the MOUPP, including the
coordination of MOHPP staff with the staff of other ministries at the field
level, will be initiated with the help of consultants by July 31, 1975; the
detailed terms of reference of the study shall be acceptable to IDA (Annex 4)
and the results of the study shall be submitted to IDA by December 31, 1975.
Thereafter the Government shall implement such recommendations within a period
to be agreed upon between the Government and IDA. The whole organizational
structure of the program now being established will be reviewed one year
after credit effectiveness bv the Government and by IDA to determine if any
changes seem desirable to accomplish the purpose of the project.
3.04      For implementation of the program in the individual ministries the
project requires the appointment of a population program officer (PPO) in
each of the five non-health ministries involved in the population program.
The PPOs will be given training abroad for three months. Training will com-
mence with a two-week orientation in Dacca (IDA will provide a consultant to
help design the course), followed by a week's visit to the thanas where the
schemes will eventually be working. The appointment of the PPOs with
qualifications and experience acceptable to IDA and the making of arrangements
for their training abroad will be a condition of credit effectiveness.
3.05      The Five-Year Plan proposes the establishment of a high-level
National Population Council (NPC) with the Prime Minister as the Chairman
for policy-making and program guidance. No decision has yet been taken on
its establishment. The establishment of the NPC with substantially the
composition and functions set out in the Plan is also a condition of credit
effectiveness.
3.06      A great deal of administrative efficiency results from strong
political commitment. A seminar on population problems for political leaders
is included in the project for the purpose of obtaining political support to
the population planning program. About 150 participants are expected to
participate in a four-day seminar that is to be organized by the Bangladesh
Institute of Development Studies with technical support supplied by the PPD.
Training Facilities for Health and Population Planning Personnel
3.07      College of Nursing:  The present national total of 40 tutors to
handle all training of nurses, FWVs and midwives falls far short of the
requirement. The tutor training program has already started on a modest
scale (20 students per year) using make-shift quarters that are not suitable
for the size of the program needed. The project will replace the existing
facility with a new facility capable of enrolling 60 new nurse-tutor students
per year for a two-year course of training. While the ultimate use of the
building will be as a training college for public health nurses, the present



- 12 -
shortage of nursing teachers is so severe that the primary use of the facility
until 1983 will be to train instructors. After 1983, the major use of the
building will shift to the training of public health nurses. The avail-
ability and need for nursing tutors over the next decade is shown in Annex 5.
Although the college will have a capacity of 120 students, 40 of these are
expected to be in the field at any given time so that the buildings will be
designed for a capacity of 80 students. The component includes construction,
equipment and furniture for teaching and dormitory accommodations for 80
students. The buildings will be located in Dacca on a site at or near an
existing hospital. One sedan and three micro-buses for field training will
also be provided. Construction is scheduled for completion in early 1978.
Special equipment will be provided by UNICEF and technical assistance by WHO.
3.08      Family Welfare Visitor Training Schools:  The earlier separate
family planning program relied on lady family planning visitors (LFPVs) who
were trained for 15 months and were responsible for IUD insertions. This
category is likely to be discontinued now under the integrated program and
the existing LFPVs will be retrained as family welfare visitors (FWVs). (Chart
8949) The FWVs are the medical auxiliaries who provide family planning services
at peripheral health units, rural health complexes and union health sub-
centers. It is also intended that the FWVs provide cotnseling and motivation
services at the centers, perform IUD insertions, screen clients for contra-
indications before dispensing pills, provide follow-up services for clients,
and supervise the female family welfare workers (FFWWs). To perform both
the in-house and area supervisory functions it is planned to staff each thana
health complex with two FWVs (the present staffing pattern provides for only
one). The increased staffing level plus the expansion in a number of health
centers is expected to raise the need for FWVs from the present number of
600 to about 1,500 by 1978 and to 2,500 by 1983 (Annex 5). These demands cannot
be met from the three existing schools (two of which are in make-shift rented
quarters) which have a combined annual output of 50 per year. The project,
therefore, includes the construction, equipping, and furnishing of eight FWVs
training schools at different locations. Each school will provide classroom
and dormitory space for 90 students composed of three classes of 30 each
admitted at six-month intervals. The MOHPP has reduced the present training-
time for FWVs from 27 to 18 months, which will increase the capacity of training
facilities by one-third. The reduction in training time is achieved by making
the training more intensive without sacrificing the quality. The project's
eight schools and a shortened training-time will expand the present annual
output of FWVs from about 50 per year at present to over 400 per year from
1979 onwards. This output would be just adequate to meet the demand of 1,500
nurses in 1978 and 2,500 in 1985. (Annex 5) Before completion of the new
schools the Government should expand FWV training, using four existing regional
Training-cum-Research Institutes (TCRIs) that were functioning for LFPV
training or through comparable facilities. Agreement has been reached during
negotiations that the Government will submit to IDA a program, including a
time schedule for its implementation to: (a) ensure recruitment of required
number of candidates for FWV training, (b) minimize the attrition rate during



13 
training, and (c) ensure that those who complete their training are thereafter
hired and provided adequate incentives to remain in their jobs. Agreement
has been reached further that the Government will carry out a manpower
utilization study by December 1976 to (a) determine what functions are being
performed by FWVs, (b) assess the suitability of training, including curricula,
in terms of intended functions and (c) to recommend any reallocation of func-
tions which may be required. The Government will implement changes in training
and/or functions of the staff in the light of this study in consultation with
IDA according to a timetable acceptable to IDA.
3.09      Field Training Thana Health Complexes:  The project does not aim at
any major extension of the rural health infrastructure. It does, however,
provide construction of a limited number of thana health complexes that can
be used as centers for field training of new health personnel. To this end,
the project includes construction, equipping, and furnishing of 8 new thana
health complexes. Each of these complexes will have four components (a) an
outpatient rural health center, (b) a 25-bed maternity ward unit, (c) a
dormitory for 25 students and (d) staff housing. Components (b) and (c)
make these complexes different from the standard thana health centers the
Government has proposed to construct. These 8 field demonstration complexes
would be located in areas accessible to FWV schools and medical colleges.
One main function of these complexes will be to provide field training
opportunities for medical and paramedical personnel in community medicine
with emphasis on maternal and child health, family planning and pediatric
medicine. A group of about 15 medical students would spend a minimum of two
weeks in these complexes during one of the clinical years. Also, FWV trainees
(10 in each group) would have eight to ten weeks of field practice in these
complexes in order to complete the required number of delivery attendances,
in addition to cases of IUD insertions, before becoming qualified. The
occupancy rate in the existing six-bed centers is low, but with provision
of kitchen facilities, ambulance, and ward attendants the occupancy rate
should increase. It is expected then that the 25-bed maternity ward units
will provide an adequate number of cases for delivery-care training. Agree-
ment has been reached during negotiations that the Government shall take all
necessary action to increase the demand for beds for maternity, gynecological,
sterilization and similar cases and insure the availability of such beds
whenever there is demand for such purposes. An annual total of 180 medical
students and 60 FWVV trainees are expected to go through field training at
each of these field training centers after their completion in 1978. These
centers will also be used for refresher courses for female family welfare
workers whom the MOIPPP is planning to appoint. (See para 3.12.) The numbers
to be trained at the field demonstration centers are given in Annex 5.
3.10      Field Training at Health Subcenters:  For further field training in
rural areas the project will build, equip, and furnish 24 health subcenters
at the union level - three attached to each of the eight thana health complexes
described above. The subcenters will have outpatient clinic facilities plus



- 14 -
housing accommodations for key staff and for two trainees (medical students
and FWVs). It is expected that these subcenters would provide general out-
patient health care including IUD insertions and vasectomies. The existing
186 subcenters are temporarily located in rented premises. The operation
of these 24 new subcenters will provide experience for the later expansion
of the system.
3.11      Model Family Planning Clinics:  No facilities exist at present for
the training of medical students in new contraceptive technology and modern
methods of induced abortion or for clinical research activities to provide
up-to-date material for teaching. The project includes the construction,
equipping, and furnishing of four model family planning clinics at four of
the country's eight medical colleges. The project will also provide two
microbuses for each clinic. The project financing will include the cost
of the above facilities and the operating cost for two years. The clinics
will have facilities for sterilization, abortion, and outpatient contraceptive
services. A ward with 25 beds will be attached to the clinic for abortion and
sterilization cases. The addition of these wards would help to meet the
growing demand for tubectomies as cases now have to wait for a number of
months due to the shortage of beds in the obstetrics and gynecology sections
of the hospitals. It is expected that each of the model clinics could
annually perform about 2,000 male and female sterilizations, 600 to 800
abortions, and would provide two weeks in-service training for 50 qualified
doctors per year in addition to undergraduate training (Annex 6).
Services
3.12      Support for Female Family Welfare Workers:  As mentioned earlier
the MOHPP has decided to extend basic health and family planning services
at the village level throughout the country by appointing two-person teams
of multipurpose workers (one man, and one woman) to be designated as family
welfare workers (FWWs). They would be responsible for inoculations,
maternal and child health and family planning services, sanitation, and
simple health tasks, including record-keeping, in addition to health and
family planning education. The Ministry will begin by trying to put in
place some 12,000 teams by 1978, one for every 6,000 people (the ultimate
objective is one for every 4,000 people). Formerly, there were on the
Government payroll about 10,000 single-purpose health workers (most of them
underemployed malaria eradication and family planning workers); these have
been redesignated and retrained as FWWS for health services in rural areas.
All but 500 of the 12,000 family welfare workers are males. Thus, there
remains a major recruitment and training problem for the female side of the
FWW teams. The provision of MCH/EP education and services will depend almost
exclusively on the Female Family Welfare Worker (FFWW). The project would
provide salaries and training costs for 3,700 FFWWs for four years. The
UNFPA will provide similar support for an additional 1,800 such workers, if
the Government provides 2,000 more, there will be 7,500 FFWWs or two per each



union by the end of the First Five-Year Plan period. It is expected that
by 1981 all the required 12,000 females would be recruited and the number
would increase to 15,000 by 1983, that would mean achieving the Governments
goal of four teams of FWWs per union. Details on the qualifications and
functions of FFWWs will be found in Annex 7. Agreement has been reached
during negotiations on the job description of these FFWWs which should allow
them to spend at least half of their time on family planning motivation and
services. Further agreement has been reached that the program of recruitment
and training of FWVs and the study of manpower utilization mentioned in
para. 3.08 would cover FFWWs as well.
Information, Education, and Motivation Components
3.13      Overview:  The project includes a major effort to launch activities
designed to increase public understanding, in the rural areas, of the country's
population problems and of the personal advantages and possibilities of
fertility regulation. This is being attempted by integrating family planning
education and motivation efforts with the development programs of five dif-
ferent ministries in addition to the MOHPP. This approach will have a dual
impact by conveying the idea of family planning through programs that already
have gained sufficient public confidence and which also encourage social
development conducive to fertility reduction. For example, one of the most
widely-accepted hypotheses is that changes in the traditional status and roles
of women lead to greater interest in fertility control. Four of the nine
pilot schemes are specially designed to further such changes. And since the
men's attitudes to family planning are of great importance, other schemes
are designed to gain their support through various male-oriented development
programs. The pilot schemes are intended to cover a wide geographic area
and a variety of socio-economic demographic and administrative settings.
One thana from each of the 19 districts has been selected for most of the
schemes. Four thanas will implement almost all the pilot schemes; these
will provide experience on the effect of a concentrated and combined operation.
The rest of the thanas will have separate schemes which will make it possible
to evaluate their impact. About 12.6 million or 17% of the total population
will be covered during the life of the project. Details of geographic cover-
age and a list of pilot thanas are given in Annex 8 and are shown on the map.
In addition to the nine pilot schemes the project will include activities
designed to strengthen the capacity of (a) the Ministry of Information and
Broadcasting to prepare and disseminate population material through the mass
media, and (b) the Ministry of Education so that it can introduce population
education into the school curriculum. Agreement has been reached during
negotiations that before starting on the implementation of pilot schemes
the Government will send to IDA the training program of personnel, supervision
procedures and information on the population to be reached by such schemes.
The paragraphs that follow describe the pilot schemes and other activities
of each of the participating ministries.
3.14      Ministry of Local Government, Rural Development and Cooperatives:
The Ministry's integrated rural development programs (IRDP) depend heavily



- 16 -
on local institutions and leadership, such as elected managers of the village
cooperative societies and enterprising farmers known as model farmers. The
project will make it possible for cooperative managers and model farmers in
19 pilot thanas to receive education in family planning so that they may be
able to talk to organized groups or to arrange for groups to listen to pro-
grams from Radio Bangladesh that would include population and family planning
topics. In addition, the Ministry is planning to experiment with the mem-
bership of women in cooperatives which will not only mobilize small savings
and provide small loans but which will also organize the women's functional
literacy program. In addition to reading and writing, these programs will
include simple training in sewing, poultry farming, and home development,
including family planning and kitchen gardening.   Five women from each
selected village of the same pilot thanas interested in these fields will
be trained at the thana level for the purpose of returning to organize
literacy and training classes in their own villages. Both cooperative
managers and organizers of women's programs will be responsible for the
distribution of contraceptives such as condoms and other methods at sub-
sidized rates. Project support will consist of needed equipment including
radios, incremental recurring costs for three years, including salary support
for a population program officer, an assistant population officer at head-
quarters and supervisory staff at the thana level, advisory services plus
foreign training, and an internal evaluation. (Annex 9)
3.15      Ministry of Labor and Social Welfare:  The Ministry's existing
social services program relies on encouraging the formation of local vol-
untary groups for rendering various types of social services. The project
will support three types of activities in the Ministry. The first is the
formation of mothers' clubs in each of the 760 villages of the 19 pilot thanas.
These clubs will engage in functional literacy activities similar to those
planned by the women's village cooperatives in the IRDP areas. These clubs
will largely reach wives of landless laborers while the wives of those with
landholdings will be reached through the cooperative program described in the
preceding paragraph. The activity will be carried out by the Rural Social
Services Division in the Ministry's Directorate of Social Welfare, which will
add a population program officer to assist the Director. The project will in-
clude necessary additional equipment, incremental recurring costs for three
years, including the salary support for the population program officer and
other staff, foreign training, advisory services and internal evaluation.
The second is the expansion of urban vocational training activities to rural
women through the National Rehabilitation Board, a semi-autonomous affiliate
of the Ministry. These activities have purposes and content similar to the
functional literacy programs of village women's cooperatives and the mothers'
clubs. But they will cover entirely different thanas not reached by the
other two programs and have a more formal employment-oriented approach than
the other two schemes. The Board already has such a scheme in operation at
32 centers and has been extremely successful in giving eight-week training



- 17 -
courses in family planning and engaging the women in gainful economic activity.
The project includes additional equipment and incremental recurring costs
for three years in addition to an internal evaluation of the scheme. The
third type of program relates to a population program for organized labor.
The Ministry operates 18 labor welfare centers in urban areas where large-
scale industries exist. These centers have health clinics with medical
officers but there has not in the past been any educational activity or
services offered in the field of family planning. The project will introduce
such activities at three labor welfare centers by making available to indus-
trial laborers and their families education about family planning and ser-
vices at the clinic, plus a system of home follow-up visits. These new
activities will require the addition of auxiliary medical personnel and
motivators/home visitors at the clinics, plus the appointment of a population
program officer to help the Labor Directorate carrv out the program. The
project will cover necessary additional equipment, incremental recurring costs
for three years including salary support for the population program officer
and other staff, foreign training, advisory services and an internal evalua-
tion. (Annex 10) The UNFPA is providing assistance similar to this component
for three additional labor welfare centers and for the introduction of
population education into the courses conducted at the Industrial Relations
Institute at Tongi.
3.16      Ministry of Agriculture:  The Agricultural Extension Service is
the only Government service that has assistants in all unions. An attempt
will be made to train these agricultural assistants in the 19 pilot thanas
in population and family planning matters. These thanas would be the same
as those from which mothers' clubs will be selected. It is expected that
about 200 of these assistants would then introduce family-size considerations
into the advice they give to the farmers with whom they work. Project support
will provide incremental recurring costs for three years including salary
support for the population program officer and supervisorv personnel at the
regional level, bicycles and motivation kits for assistants, foreign training
and an internal evaluation. In addition, the Agricultural Information Service
will be given funds and equipment to enable it to produce a series of film
slides on population matters both for the Ministry's own use and for other
ministries assisted by the project. (Annex 11)
3.17      Ministry of Education:  The teachers in the villages are considered
to be respected elite who can legitimize development programs. Their assist-
ance in community education on population problems can be valuable. The
project will therefore, provide orientation courses in population problems
for teachers of both primary and secondary schools in 19 pilot thanas through
mobile population education teams. These thanas will be in common with
the scheme using FFWWs. (para 3.24) The project provides for a population
program officer, his study tour abroad, advisory services needed, equipment
and incremental recurring costs for three years and an internal evaluation.
In addition, the project also includes incremental recurring costs for
training of primary and secondary school teachers and curriculum development



- 18 -
for the introduction of population education into primary and secondary
schools. (Annex 12) Agreement has been obtained during negotiations that
population education will systematically be introduced in to the regular
primary and secondary school curricula by January 1979. Furthermore, the
Government will send to IDA, according to the agreed timetable, the proposed
curricula and implementation schedule for the introduction of population
education in various grades.
3.18      Ministry of Information and Broadcasting (MIB):  The Ministry will
conduct one pilot scheme in eight thanas that relates to the use of mobile
audiovisual vans for the showing of films, slides, film-strips, and other
audiovisual materials dealing with population and family planning. The
project will provide eight closed-door and eight open-air cinema vans with
the necessary equipment, an audiovisual engineer and other personnel, in
addition to other recurring expenses for three years. Central responsibility
for this component will rest with the population program officer at head-
quarters; at the thana level, with the Ministry's regular public relations
officer and the Department of Mass Communication at the Ministry. (Annex 13).
3.19      In addition to the pilot scheme, four institution-building com-
ponents for the Ministry of Information and Broadcasting (described in later
sections) are included in the project. At present this important Ministry'
gives almost no attention to population matters and has little or no present
capacity to do so. The project will introduce new organizational units into
the Ministry so that it can become an important institutional resource for
disseminating information and creating awareness of national population
problems through the mass media. The creation of awareness by the mass media
is a vital background activity that greatly heightens the effectiveness of
other, more specific information and additional program activities. All
these population activities in various departments of the Ministry will be
coordinated and monitored by the population program officer.
3.2n      The Establishment of a Population Program Cell in Radio Bangladesh
for the production of population programs: Radio was the most effective
communication medium during the War of Independence. The country is relatively
well covered with broadcasting stations and receiving sets. A national sta-
tion in Dacca broadcasts 14-15 hours per (lay; there are also five regional
stations. The project will add personnel and equipment at Dacca and at the
five other stations for the purpose of preparing and airing population
programs. About 1,900 radio sets will be provided to the model farmers and
cooperative managers to test the effectiveness of these programs. Agreement
has been reached during negotiations that (a) appropriate records, satisfactory
to IDA, of national radio time devoted to the population program will be
maintained, and (b) the new staff would be in their posts within three
months of credit effectiveness. If the equipment has not yet arrived at
that time they will make interim use of whatever equipment may be available.



- 19 -
3.21      Strengthening the Ministry's Film Department:  Before Independence
the Films Department made some family planning films but nothing has been
produced since then. At present production is concentrated on newsreels and
major documentaries on national development. There is neither the staff nor
the equipment needed to produce the number of films required to give continuing
support to the project. The project provides necessary equipment and support
for personnel in the Films Department for three years to enable them to
produce at least fourteen short films and one feature-length film in 35 mm
with subsequent reduction to 16 mm. From the date of credit effectiveness,
and pending the arrival of the essential equipment, the Films Department will
put at least one film into production with their existing facilities and
prepare for the production of three more. If, at the end of the first year,
because of technical problems, the Department will not be able to make all
fourteen films, it will contract out to commercial producers who will work
under the supervision of the Department. To keep the projectors provided
under the project supplied with films during the period which will elapse
before the new equipment is installed, the Films Department will contract
the dubbing into Bengali of six existing and suitable family planning films.
3.22      Training of Public Relations Officers:  The Ministry has about 90
public relations officers (posted at the district and sub-divisional level)
in the Department of Mass Communication. These officers will be brought to
Dacca in groups of 15-20 for a two-week orientation in population problems,
policies, programs, and motivation techniques. The project funds will cover
a training officer at the Department of Mass Communication, honoraria for
lectures, travel allowance for three years and office equipment. The
training officer will be responsible for organizing the program.
3.23      Population Feature Writing Bureau:  The Population Feature Writing
Bureau will be established within the Ministry's Press Information Department.
The Bureau will write articles, features, personal interviews on population
subjects for use in newspapers, magazines, radio (support to the radio cell)
and TV. An important purpose of the Bureau will be to serve as a source of
ideas for the production of radio programs, films, etc., elsewhere in the
Ministry; hence its establishment will be a priority task. The project will
include camera, office equipment and incremental recurring costs for three
years for the Bureau. Agreement has been reached during negotiations that the
Bureau will go into full action within eight months of credit effectiveness.
3.24      M1inistry of llealth and Population Planning:  The Female Family Welfare
Workers at the MOHPP are responsible for the delivery of family planning ser-
vices as well as for face-to-face family planning education. (para 3.12)
The MOHPP will provide them with a kit largely for delivery of services; these
kits will be financed by the MOHPP or with WHo support. The project provides
for kits containing tape recorders, slide projectors and flip charts to give
audiovisual support to the family planning talks given by the FFWWs in the
19 pilot thanas. (Annex 14)



- 20 -
Evaluation and Research
3.25      Evaluation:  Evaluation of all program components is crucial at
thls stage of program development. The system of evaluation to be established
would attempt to measure the (a) total program performance in terms of
decline in age specific and total fertility rates and (b) effectiveness of the
program components in terms of their impact on knowledge, attitude and prac-
tice towards family planning. (Annex 15) To measure total program per-
formance, the MOHPP has instituted a system of vital registration as a part
of the couple registration scheme and also of keeping the basic records of
contraceptive acceptors. The Family Welfare Workers are to collect these
data in the field. No system of analysis and feedback of this information
has so far been designed. The project includes services of an expatriate
adviser, salaries of additional manpower and other operating costs for
the assessment of this system, establishment of a system of flow of such
information, analysis of data and feedback of results. These data would
not only help in analyzing the total program performance but also in assessing
the effectiveness of the system of delivery of services as they would contain
acceptor records by method. In addition, special surveys are included in
the project to obtain information on continuation rates of contraceptive
methods and on demographic parameters to supplement the regular reporting
system described above. To measure the effectiveness of individual program
components, the project includes special studies to review the delivery
of family planning services under the integrated plan and also to review
the functions of the FWVs and FFWWs. The evaluation of pilot schemes would
be divided into two parts (a) routine reporting on the progress of the schemes
and special studies and (b) base-line and follow-up surveys for measuring
the knowledge, attitude and practice of family planning. The project includes
additional staff, operating costs for three years and calculating machines
for routine reporting and special studies. In addition, for base-line studies
and follow-up surveys the project provides funds at the PPD and the PPS. The
evaluation of the pilot schemes and the system of service statistics are
described in Annexes 16 and 17.
3.26      Within the Health and Population Planning Section (PPS) there will
be an Evaluation Unit which will have responsibility for conducting follow-up
surveys and other studies for external evaluations (evaluation outside the
implementing ministry). The PPS will have a core staff and funds to contract
out to various universities and research institutes the studies that it
cannot conduct. Regular reporting and base-line surveys would be the respon-
sibility of the implementing ministries who would collect information, collate
it and send it to the PPD for analysis. These ministries would also be
responsible for feedback to the staff and improvements in the operations of
the scheme. The PPD will have an evaluation section (with the existing
resources at MOHPP) and the functions of t;,is section would be to (a) arrange
the base-line surveys for internal evaluation of pilot schemes as well as for
the delivery of services, in addition to a health and family planning manpower
utilization study through its staff, (b) analyze the reports received from



- 21 -
all implementing ministries, including the service statistics from the MOHPP,
and (c) provide feedback on the performance of the program to the concerned
ministries and to the coordination committees at all levels.
3.27      The Evaluation Units of the PPD and the PPS, with the assistance
of an adviser (provided in the project), would also lay down the standard of
performance for the program and its individual components. The evaluation
would attempt to (a) analyze the differential performance of the pilot
schemes, delivery of services and other program activities by geographic
area to determine the impact of social, cultural, economic, political and
administrative factors on the success of the scheme and (b) determine to what
extent the services provided by the program correspond with the wishes of the
community and what type of changes are needed to make the services more accept-
able. A schedule of different studies has been prepared and is given in
Annex 15. Agreement has been reached during negotiations on the schedule of
completion of these studies.
3.28      Research:  A Population Studies Center (PSC) has been established
at the Bangladesh Institute of Development Studies. The Ford Foundation and
the Population Council are likely to be heavily involved in the development
of PSC's research program and in assisting the PSC with technical assistance
and equipment to carry out the program to a large extent. However, a study
of the determinants of reproductive behavior could have an important bearing
on the development of a long-term population policy and should get priority.
A Bank consultant is preparing detailed plans for such a study. The study
will be designed to provide information about people's attitudes to family
planning. This will be essential for the information and motivation program
of the project. The project includes technical assistance, equipment and
local costs for conducting such a study in case the expected private source
funds should not be forthcoming in adequate amounts. Annex 18 gives the
details of the study. Agreement has been reached during negotiations that
this study will be completed by June 1978. It is not known what effect, if
any, existing national laws may have on the size of families couples choose
to have. An examination of the effect of national laws on population growth
might reveal desirable or undesirable biases which should be strengthened
or changed. It is therefore recommended that the PPD establish a committee
that will commission a study of the national laws for its impact. The study
might include such topics as tax law, property law (including the law of
inheritance), family law (including the lawful age at marriage), criminal
law (including e.g., law of abortion) and similar subjects. (Annex 18)
Agreement has been reached during negotiations that the terms of reference
of this study will be acceptable to IDA and shall inter alia include con-
sideration of changing the law relating to the minimum age for lawful marriage.
The submission of the terms of the study is a condition of credit effectiveness.
Further, agreement was reached that the recommendations of the study will
be submitted to the NPC by December 31, 1976.



- 22 -
Other Activities
3.29      Government Support for the Private Sector and Innovative Activities:
The role of the Family Planning Association (a voluntary organization) in
the national program is limited at this time. The Government should make an
extended effort to encourage and support voluntary organizations actively
participating in the national program as they have been found to be useful in
other countries. It is impossible to identify in advance all the program
activities that deserve to be taken up during the period of the project. While
some attractive future program ideas may find alternative means of support,
it is far simpler and more appropriate for the project to recognize the nature
of program evolution in this sector and to make specific provision for desirable
flexibility and spontaneity. The project funding therefore includes US$350,000
to support the activities of voluntary organizations and innovative activities
both in the private and public sectors. Agreement has been reached during
negotiations on the establishment within three months of credit effectiveness
of a Subventions Committee within the PPD to approve schemes to be financed
under this component (at least half the funds provided for these activities
under the project will be allocated for the private sector). Furthermore,
agreement has been reached during negotiations that all proposals for the
use of project funds for these two purposes should be agreed upon in advance
with IDA.
D. Prolect Implementation and Administration
Detailed Preparation
3.30      All project activities are scheduled for completion within four
years, e.g., by 1979 (assuming project activities start in 1975), The two
major project activities (the construction program and the pilot schemes)
will both require prompt and energetic attention to preparatory activities
if these components are to be completed within the scheduled four-year period.
The Government faces a major task of identifying construction locations, of
identifying, acquiring, and surveying building sites, of preparing standards,
functional programs and architectural designs for project facilities, of
working out construction program details and methods of controlling its
costs, and of assuring the availability of essential supplies. While the
individual buildings are simple ones, the construction program as a whole
is much larger and more complex than the MOHPP has ever carried out before.
To maintain an effective time and cost control of the program, the BPDU and
its consultants (para 3.36) will prepare a Master Plan for implementation of
the construction part. Agreement has been reached during negotiations that
(a) a Master Plan will be prepared by December 15, 1975, (b) all pilot schemes
will be in launched in the first-half of 1975 and (c) the contents of family
planning training and education of the staff in the pilot schemes and that
of the population to be covered will be sent to IDA for its comments before
initiation of the scheme.



- 23 -
lzcations and Sites
3.31      The project will require that the Government have or acquire
about 45 building sites with suitable locational and technical charac-
teristics. (see Map) The early identification and acquisition of these
sites is essential to the preparation of final designs and to the orderly
and economical "packaging" of construction contracts. At present, the Govern-
ment has supplied a list of locations of all project facilities (see Annex 19),
but no information is available on any individual sites. During negotiations
agreement has been reached on a set of guidelines for the selection of sites.
Agreement was also reached that sites for the college of nursing and the fa-
mily welfare visitors' training schools will be acquired and in the Govern-
ment's possession not later than December 31, 1975, and for all facilities
not later than April 30, 1976. A condition of effectiveness is the submission
to IDA of a description of the proposed sites for the facilities, indicating,
in the case of the nursing college and the FWV training schools, the sites'
boundaries and approximate areas, and in the case of all facilities whether
the sites have been acquired by the Government and if they have not, the steps
already taken, and those proposed to acquire them.
Project Administration
3.32      Project administration will rely primarily on existing agencies
within the Government (Chart 9488); no new entity for project administration
or implementation will be created except for the Building Planning and Design
Unit (BPDU) in the MOHPP. The BPDU, along with the Building Directorate,
will have major responsibility for the implementation of the construction
component of the project. Implementation of all non-construction components
will be the responsibility of the respective ministries which will maintain p
progress reports and conduct internal evaluation. Five participating minis-
tries will appoint population program officers for the purpose (see para 3.35).
The newly established PPD in the M0HPP in collaboration with PPS in the
Planning Commission will have responsibility for supervising, monitoring,
coordinating and evaluating project activities. The PPD will g-iide and review
the activities of different ministries, provide technical assistance for the
internal evaluation and conduct or arrange for training of involved personnel
from other ministries. The PPS will be responsible for the external evalua-
tion of project activities and allocation of funds. The PPS will also be
the central point of contact and reporting between IDA and the Government
on project implementation. (Annex 20)
3.33      For purposes of centralizing project financial responsibility and
preparing applications for withdrawal of the proceeds of the credit, a
special project finance officer shall be designated by the Government (Ministry
of Finance) and assigned to the PPD. This officer will have adequate staff
to conduct an independent inspection regarding the use of personnel, equipment
and material in the field before submitting the receipts for final reimburse-
