ment. (Annex 20) Appointment of the project's financial officer at the
PPD is a condition of credit effectiveness.



- 24 -
3.34      There are important communication elements in all of the sections
of the project which are being looked after by the six ministries. In order
that these will form part of a communication strategy and that they are
properly evaluated, the PPS will form study groups in at least two key areas
of project activity where the knowledge, understanding and cooperation of
individuals outside the PPS will be important. One working group will be
concerned with information, education, and communication. It will consist
of PPOs from the implementing ministries plus a few other experts (proposed
terms of reference for this group appear in Annex 20). A second group will
be formed to advise on all evaluation and research activities. It will
consist of representatives of universities and research institutions (terms
of reference are in Annex 20). Agreement has been reached during negotiations
on the establishment of these working groups by the PPS by July 1975.
3.35      Population Program Officers:  Among the individual ministries the
proposed population program officers will be responsible for the implement-
ation of specific project components allocated to their respective ministries
except for the Health Division of the MOHPP. In the case of the MOHPP (Health
Division) the Joint Secretary, Family Planning (or equivalent officer) under
the Secretary, will be responsible for the project component under it.
3.36      Construction:  The BPDU is intended to be a small, full-time
technical/expediting group within the MOHPP. Its primary responsibilities
will be to prepare, expedite, and to monitor the construction program, to
maintain project construction accounts, to prepare periodic progress reports,
and to plan the commissioning of each facility. The unit's suggested terms
of reference are in Annex 20 and include the responsibility for the establish-
ment of standards, the preparation of functional programs, and design of the
buildings including construction methods. This standards-setting function
will specifically, among other things, include a review of the Government's
present design to build eight two-story 25-bed thana health centers to see
if an acceptable alternative design (including building materials and methods)
can be devised, which would be better adapted to future replication in the
light of the country's economic circumstances. Preliminary drawings and the
outline of specifications for all project buildings will be furnished to IDA
by September 30, 1975 and these will be subject to IDA's approval. The BPDU
should be headed by a senior civil servant or an experienced architect.
Other staff would include a project architect, a senior engineer, a project
construction accountant, a procurement officer for equipment, furniture, and
supplies, and necessary office staff. The MOIPP has already established the
BPDU by the appointment of its head (a medical professor) and a senior engineer
seconded from the Ministry of Works. The provision of the BPDU with full-
time staff, consultants and facilities acceptable to IDA is a condition of
effectiveness.
3.37      To enable the BPDU to carry out its responsibilities it should
be supported by specialists experienced in project management and architect-
ural design. This assistance can best be supplied by the engagement of a



-25 -
private consulting firm experienced in project management. These project
management consultants 1/ would, in turn, be required to retain the services
of one or more executive architects, with customary engineering and allied
technical support, if they do not have these skills within their own organiza-
tion. (Annex 20) The project management services could be supplied by a
local firm possibly supported by an experienced foreign firm, perhaps under
the kind of joint-venture arrangement that has already been used in the
country. The engagement of the project management consultant acceptable to
IDA is a condition of credit effectiveness.
3.38      There are three topics affecting the use and life of program
physical facilities which give cause for concern. These are (a) the weakness
of building maintenance activities, which threatens to shorten building life,
(b) the weakness of procedures for procuring and distributing essential
supplies which, when shortages occur, makes it impossible to offer services
for which buildings are designed, and (c) uncertainty as to the suitability
of building design until this is tested by use. These topics will be the
subject of modest studies at appropriate periods during the project's life
for which provision is made in the project. (Annex 20)
IV. COSTS, FINANCING, PROCUREMENT AND DISBURSEMENTS
Costs
4.01      Total project cost, including contingencies, is estimated at
US$45.7 million equivalent. Foreign exchange components represent 53% of
the total cost. Construction will account for nearly 60% of total costs
and the non-construction about 40%, excluding contingencies. Two-thirds
of the non-construction cost would be for support to the pilot education and
motivation programs and for the village-level female family welfare workers.
Contingencies (price escalation and unforeseen) constitute 38% of the total
project costs. The costs by major functional categories (i.e., output) are
given below; Annexes 21 and 22 provide more detailed information.
1/ This firm would not have responsibility for the entire project but only
for construction components in accordance with its terms of reference.

4.03      The cost estimates are based primarily on estimates prepared by
the responsible ministries, checked with experienced representatives of the
private sector and up-dated to July, 1974 mainly on the basis of a report
analyzing prices obtained from the Price Section of the Bangladesh Bureau
of Statistics, and on assumed levelling-out of construction price escala-
tion. Actual escalation was about 50% in 1972, 25% in the first half of
1973, and another 35% in the second half of 1973. Estimated escalation in
the first half of 1974 was 18%, and in the second half 17%. The assumed
escalation factor for the year 1975 is 19%, for 1976 14%, and for 1977 and
1978 12% each. A physical contingency of 10% has been added to the base-
line cos: estimate (July 1974) to cover unforeseen costs during the execution
of the project. The construction cost estimates made allowance for regional
cost difference. The cost estimates for special equipment and vehicles are
based on the catalogue prices for 1974 of major equipment firms. Salary
levels are based on present salary structures and an allowance of 25%
is made for salary adjustments and the need for appointments to be made at
higher levels. The operating cost i.e., vehicle maintenance, printing of
material etc., were estimated by the Government. A cost escalation of 11%
in 1975 and 7.5% per year in 1976 and 1977 is assumed for vehicles, equipment
and operating cost. For salaries the escalation is assumed to be 5.5% for
1975 and 3.5% per year for 1976 to 1978. About 10% is allowed on the
non-construction cost of the project for unforeseen contingencies. The
details of estimation of contingencies are given in Annex 23 with the
expenditure schedules.
Project Financing
4.04      The total project cost (US$45.7 million) is to be financed by an
IDA credit of US$15 million equivalent and US$30.7 million from various
donors and the Government. Tentative commitments received from various donors
are: The Norwegian Agency for International Development (NORAD - NKr 45.0
million), Federal Republic of Germany - DM 15.0 million, Government of
Australia (GOA) - A$2.0 million, British Overseas Development Ministry (ODM)
- # 1.4 million; Canadian International Development Agency (CIDA) - CN$2.0
million, and Swedish International Development Authority (SIDA) - SKr 15.0
million. A total of about US$25.4 million is expected to be available from
these donors. The Government's contribution is therefore estimated at US$5.3
million. The Government of Australia has decided to enter into joint finan-
cing arrangements with IDA for its contribution. All other donors except
SIDA would participate in the project on a parallel basis (financing identi-
fiable components) but would like IDA to assist in managing and supervising
the project. SIDA has indicated interest in joint financing arrangements
with the Government. The size of each donor's contribution and preferences
for financing different components are given in Annex 24.



- 29 -
Disbursements
4.05      Funds from the credit, and any other project funds which IDA may
be responsible for disbursing on behalf of other donors, will be disbursed
on the basis of satisfactory evidence of expenditures submitted to IDA by the
project financial officer in the PPD. Disbursements from the IDA credit will
be made as follows: (a) civil works - 60% of total expenditures; (b) equip-
ment, furniture and vehicles - 100% of the CIF cost, or 70% of local expendi-
ture, if locally procured; (c) technical assistance and overseas training -
100% of foreign expenditures; (d) operating costs of the PPD, PPS, MIB, MOHPP
and BIDS - 100% of total expenditures; and (e) support to the private sector
and innovative activities - 100% of total expenditures. The aggregate of the
Australian grant will be apportioned among the same categories of expenditure
as the IDA credit and will be disbursed at the same disbursement percentage
for each such category. The grants of CIDA, NORAD, ODM and the Federal
Republic of Germany will be disbursed at the rate of 100% of the total cost
of components allocated to the respective donor.
4.06      If actual costs exceeds the estimates, the percentages will be ad-
justed to ensure that the donors' funds are spread over the project life and
that the Government will pick up the balance. IDA will assume as much
responsibility for the disbursement of other donors' funds as the latter
may request it to assume. Specific arrangements between IDA and each donor
will be made. Each donor will conclude a separate agreement with the Govern-
ment as a condition of credit effectiveness. The schedule of expected project
expenditures and IDA disbursements, assuming reasonable project performance,
appear in Annex 23.
Incremental Recurring Cost
4.07      The incremental recurring cost of the facilities provided in the
project is estimated at US$1.4 million per annum excluding the recurring
cost of four model clinics. (Annex 25) It constitutes a small portion (6%)
of the total recurring budget of the MOHPP, which amounted to US$35 million
for 1972-73 (excluding the recurring budget of the NFPB, estimated at US$4
million). These costs are expected to form part of the MOHPP budgetary
provisions without undue strain. The project supports operation of the four
model clinics, a new type of facility estimated at US$0.4 million for two
years.
Procurement
4.08      Civil works to be executed under the project will consist of small,
widely-dispersed structures expected to range in cost from less than US$50,000
to US$1.5 million each, with a total of approximately US$13 million. Procure-
ment will be by international competitive bidding in accordance with IDA's
Guidelines. Since it is unlikely that any of the civil works contracts will



attract bidders from outside Bangladesh, advertisements will be limited to
local newspapers supplemented by notifications to local embassies. Contractors
will be prequalified. Domestic contractors will be accorded a preference of
7.5%.
4.09      IDA-financed contracts for furniture, equipment, vehicles and parts,
and imported building materials estimated to cost about US$14 million will be
awarded on the basis of international competitive bidding in accordance with
IDA's Guidelines, except for contracts of US$10,000 or less or where com-
patibility with existing items is important, which may apply to vehicles and
some of the equipment with a total estimated cost of approximately US$4.5
million. In the evaluation of bids, domestic manufacturers will be allowed
a preference margin of 15% or the existing rate of import duties, whichever
is lower.
4.10      All procurement will be based on bid packages prepared in consulta-
tion with IDA. Bidding documents for all contracts of US$50,000 or more
will be sent to IDA for review.
Revolving Fund
4.11      To speed up project payments and to encourage the prompt sub-
mission of applications for reimbursement, the Government will establish
a revolving fund, in Takas, from which will be paid approved vouchers
for (a) consultants and advisers, (b) import duties and other expenses
incidental to imports and (c) contingencies (any other expenditure for which
prompt payment is needed). The Government will pay into this fund a sum of
Takas 750,000 (US$99,000) and this fund will be maintained at least at that
level by replenishment each month. The establishment of this fund is a con-
dition of credit effectiveness.
V. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
Operational
5.01      This project is the first of its kind in IDA and in Bangladesh
involving several different ministries and agencies in an attempt to make
population planning a part of relevant development activities. Because of
its multisectoral nature and because of the special situation in the country
the project will need close supervision. A major problem is likely to be the
timely implementation of the construction program because of the shortage
of building materials in the country and delays in imports. It will be
necessary to make special arrangements for procurement of materials and con-
tinuous monitoring and follow up by IDA. Separate procurement of building



- 31 -
material for this project and placement of an officer responsible for the
project in the Bank's resident mission in Dacca may help in overcoming some
of the difficulties. The project's functional contributions will be in
the fields of training, the initiation of a fairly ambitious program of pilot
activities dealing with education and motivation, and the organization of a
set of program evaluations and demographic research studies. In particular,
the training of auxiliary health personnel and multipurpose basic health
workers at the project facilities is the core element required for increasing
future coverage of the rural population with family planning services through
the integrated health delivery system.
Demographic
5.02      It is difficult with available data at this time to make any
meaningful estimates of the project's probable demographic impact. However,
with simple methodology the fertility reduction target set in the Five-Year
Plan has been translated into an estimated number of births to be averted
and the number of acceptors required by the end of the century. It is
estimated that about 65 million births need to be averted to achieve the goal
of a two-child family by the end of the century, requiring about 60% of women
within the reproductive ages to accept family planning. The program, if it
maintains the activities generated in the project and makes full use of its
resources, can avert about 23 million births or one-third of the required
number. The project would directly avert only about 1.3 million births. This
does not include the indirect contribution of the project in generating certain
program activities that would continue far beyond its life. In addition, the
project may result in improved maternal and child health which will also con-
tribute indirectly to fertility regulation; this in itself is desirable.
The major requirements for achieving the demographic objectives in the long-
run, assuming the development of a suitable organizational structure and a
strong political commitment, would be (a) a nation-wide extension of demand-
creating activities using, inter alia, experience of the present project,
and (b) intensive coverage of the population by extending Government and
private delivery systems and the variety of methods offered.
5.03      The decline in fertility envisaged in the Plan, if achieved, would
reduce the natural rate of growth of population from 3% at present to less
than 1.5% by the end of the century, eventually achieving the goal of zero
population growth in the middle of the next century. The assumed age specific
fertility rates are shown in Chart 9096. The total population would exceed
130 million around the turn of the century, and would finally stabilize at
about 200 million in the middle of the next century. (Annex 26) These estimates
and the economic implications are referred to in Annex 1.



- 32 -
VI.  AGREEMENUS AND RECO2ThMENDATION
6.01      During negotiations agreement was reached on the following
principal points:
(a) The Government will carry out a comprehensive management
study of the MOHPP, with terms of reference acceptable
to IDA; this study will be completed by December 31, 1975
and the Government shall review the results of such a
study with IDA and implement the recommendation agreed
upon according to a time schedule determined by the
Government and IDA. (para 3.03)
(b) The Government and IDA shall review, one year after credit
effectiveness the administrative and organizational
arrangements affecting the project to determine if any
changes are desirable to accomplish the purpose of the
project. (para 3.03)
(c)  Introduction of population education as a mandatory subject
in the curricula of primary and secondary schools on a
nation-wide basis not later than January 1, 1979. (para 3.17)
(d) The Population Planning Division shall establish a committee
to supervise a study of the effect of national laws on
population growth and to make such recommendations to the
National Population Council for changes in laws as appear
appropriate by December 31, 1976. (para 3.28)
(e) The Government will acquire the land for the college of
nursing and family welfare visitors' training schools by
December 31, 1975 and for the rest of the project's
physical facilities by April 30, 1976. (para 3.31)
6.02      The conditions of credit effectiveness are:
(a) The appointment of population program officers, with
qualifications and experience acceptable to IDA in
the five concerned ministries, and arrangements for
their training abroad. (para 3.04)
(b) The establishment of the National Population Council with
functions and composition described in the country's First
Five-Year Plan. (para 3.05)
(c) Agreement on the terms of reference for the study of
national laws. (para 3.28)

BANGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Population Problems
I. Past Trends
Demographic
1.        Mortality:  The demographic history of Bangladesh, the eighth
largest country in the world with a population of 74 million, 1/ is a
reminder of the grim reality of Malthusian types of checks on population
growth in operation until recently (Table 1). While historically the natural
rate of growth of population has remained low -- at less than 1% until
1951 -- it was basically due not to low fertility, but to high mortality
(Table 2). At the present time, the natural rate of population growth is
estimated to be 3% -- which is one of the highest in Asia (Table 3). The
generally high death rate which prevailed for much of the first five decades
was periodically pushed higher by epidemic, war and famine. Mortality started
declining after World War II, and by 1969 the death rate was reduced by half,
resulting in a high natural rate of population growth (Table 4). But the
cyclone of 1970 and the War of Independence of 1971 again resulted in large
numbers of deaths whose magnitude is still a pure guess. The estimates of
casualties vary between 230,000 to 500,000 due to the cyclone,and 1.5 to 3.0
million during the war sapping the source of growth by the loss of skilled
manpower. No one knows the impact of the aftermath of the war on mortality,
but it seems that the sharp decline in mortality must have been arrested
at least temporarily in recent years. However, the mortality rate is expected
to decline again as intensive efforts are being made to restore health
services and bring the economic situation back to normal.
2.        Migration:  Apart from the low natural rate of population growth,
emigration during certain periods of demographic history of Bangladesh was
also responsible for reducing the growth rate of population although its
qualitative impact may have had an adverse effect. The net out-migration,
which has continued since 1911 largely due to the depressed economic situation,
was accelerated during the partition of India and Pakistan in the forties and
1/ The Census of February 1974 estimated the population of Bangladesh at
71.3 million. The census results are being checked for possible under-
enumeration. Taking the unadjusted population of the 1961 census at
50.8 million the rate of exponential population growth would be about
2.6% per annum between 1961-74.



A_NNEEX
?age 2
the War oL Independence recently.  About 10 millions persons were reported
to have migrated to India during the war; many have returned, but it is
estimated that no less than 0.5 - 1 million have remained in India. 1/
3.        Fertility:  As for the birth rate, there has not been a substantial
decline in it from the beginning of the century until the early fifties.
During the last two decades total fertility, according to some studies, has
fallen but its impact on the birth rate and hence on the natural rate of
population growth, at best, is indicated to be marginal 2/ (Table 5). The
extent of fertility decline and its impact on birth rate will remain con-
troversial until firm evidence, hopefully through the past census studies
is available. But one can say with certainty that irrespective of the
decline in total fertility its level is still high. Any decline in the
death rate and in the arrest of push factors for out-migratory movement
would set the potential for an unprecedented increase in population growth.
Economic
4.        Overview:  The dismally low standard of living level in Bangladesh
was clearly indicated even before the war. Half the population was on a
starvation diet, nearly 90% had some kind of nutrition deficiency and a vast
majority were ill clad and shelterless. The result was high infant mortality,
an index of malnutrition and a low expectation of life. According to one
estimate in the early sixties, about 26% of all live-born children died
before their first birthday. Those who survived suffered considerable growth
retardation. The low expectation of life at less than 47 years is in sharp
difference with 70 years in Western countries. Necessary data are not avail-
able for a detailed or consistent analysis of the magnitude of economic and
social problems created by population growth in the past. This annex, there-
fore, represents a collection of various pieces of information which
illustrate the problem.
5.        Per Capita Income:  On the basis of gross domestic product (GDP)
data at constant prices for the preceding two decades it was estimated that
the rate of population growth exceeded the rate of GDP growth, resulting
in a decline in per capita GDP at the end of the fifties. During the next
decade although the GDP was reported to have increased at double the rate
as the previous decade, no less than 70% of the increase was offset by an
acceleration in the rate of population growth during this period.
1/   The description is based on an unpublished paper on "Bangladesh:  Pop-
ulation Projections, 1961-2000", by Monnawar Hossain, IBRD, 1972.
2/   A decline in the total fertility rate is not always associated with a
corresponding decline in the birth rate because the latter depends not
only on fertility but on age and sex structure and marital patterns as
well.



ANNEX I
Page 3
6.        Food Consumption:  The possibility of a stationary or declining
standard of living during the last two decades is also indicated by the
decline in consumption of major food items between 1960-61 and 1969-70. The
per capita consumption of rice declined from 1.04 lbs per person per day
in 1960-61 to 0.96 per lbs per person per day in 1969-70. "It would be
unthinkable that the average living standards could rise without an increase
in per capita consumption of rice unless, of course, income distribution takes
a very sharp unfavorable turn. This is because at the present level of
average consumption, the income elasticity of demand for rice has been found
rather high." 1/
Education
7.        The percentage of illiteracy increased from 78.9% to 82.4% during
1951-61 even though enrollment in primary schools more than doubled. This
apparent paradox can largely be explained by the high rate of growth of
population and the high rate of school drop-outs, particularly of the girls
before acquiring literacy. Only 58% of the children of primary school
age are in schools; only 17% of 11-15 years are enrolled in the secondary
schools and about 0.1% of the population is educated to graduate or post-
graduate levels.
Land Availability
8.        A Land and Water Resource Sector Study by the Bank summarizes the
situation as follows: "The average land-owning villager possesses only 1.5
acres. He rents another acre from the well-to-do villagers and therefore
cultivates 2.5 acres. This area is usually fragmented into ten or more
plots, some of which may be a mile or more away from the homestead. He
owns one scrawny cow, used for ploughing. Most of the family resources is
the value of the rice kept for consumption. For festivals, marriages and
other social occasions he borrows from relatives, or failing that, from
moneylenders.  Since he lives from hand to mouth, he is just able to keep
up with the interest payments and hardly ever clears the principal. The
occasional bad year puts him even deeper into debt.  The life of the land-
less laborers in the villages is even more grim.  It is estimated that one-
fifth of the villagers are landless.  Their huts are of much poorer quality
and smaller than those of the landowners; the roofs are usually made of rice
straw which do not quite keep out monsoon rain. These villagers are at
the mercy of the landowners and are often evicted after a bad harvest." 2/
Unemployment
9.        [Jnemployment and underemployment are widespread in Bangladesh to-
day. While no reliable estimates are available, it is frequently suggested
1/   Kahn, A.R. - The Economy of Bangladesh, MacMillan, 1972.
2/   IBRD - Land and Water Resources Sector Study - Volume I.



AMNEXX
Page 4
that on the average up to 30 percent of the available labor is not used,
although much of this is concealed by worksharing and underemployment.
During the last decade unemployment and underemployment in Bangladesh
seems to have increased. The labor force is estimated to have increased
from 18.9 million in 1961 to 25.2 million in 1973. On the assumption of
sectoral distribution as shown in the last survey of 1967-68 it can be
concluded that about 90% of the 7.3 million increase in the labor force (or
6.5 million) was absorbed in the rural areas. Further, about 30% of this
absorption must have been in non-agricultural occupations where there is
little sign of increase in meaningful employment.
II. Future Outlook
Population Projections
10.       Two sets of population projections have been made to indicate
the most likely range within which the population level is expected to lie.
One set of projections would indicate the demographic impact of achieving
the objectives as laid down in the First Five-Year Plan (1973-78) of
reducing the birth rate to 43 in 1978 from 47 in 1973 with a long-term
goal of reaching a net reproduction rate of 1 (total fertility of 2.2 under
given mortality conditions) by the end of the century. The second set of
projections assumes a more modest goal of fertility decline so that the
total fertility is reduced to 3.4 in the year 2000. Another set of projec-
tions illustrates what would happen without an effective nationwide family
planning program. It is assumed that fertility would not remain constant
but would decline, though at a very slow rate, in response to some socio-
economic development during the next three decades.
11.       The results of these sets of assumptions are given in Table 6 and
shown in Chart 8498. The Bank's recent Economic Report (September 1974) uses
the second set of estimates for analyzing the impact of population growth on
economic development. These estimates and analysis are used here to show
the implications of population growth on basic economic variables.
Food Supply
12.       The Economic Report estimating the food requirements based on the
set of population projections mentioned above concludes that although the
projected growth in production implies the attainment of some form of food-
grain self-sufficiency, within 10-12 years, in the longer run the gap between
demand and supply will emerge once again. This is so even though demand
projections are somewhat optimistic (in that they imply a significant decline
in population growth rates) and the production projections imply levels of
implementation and efficiency never achieved in Bangladesh. The clear implica-
tion is that the substantial increases in production which might be achieved
in the seventies and eighties will only buy Bangladesh a little more time to
bring its rate of population growth under control.



ANNEX I
Page 5
Availability of Arable Land
13.       The density of population is likely to almost triple between now
and the end of the century in spite of a decline in population growth rate
assumed under the second set of projections. Further, in spite of rapid
urbanization, the availability of arable land per rural family would decline
from 2.3 acres at present to 1.8 acres in 1993.
Urbanization
14.       Assuming an increase in the percentage of urban population from
6% at present to 22% in 1993, the urban population would increase 15 times
at a minimum from over four million at present to about 25 million by 1993
an increase of 20 million population or a minimum of over three million
families. With the present situation of overcrowding in the poor dwellings
in urban areas, absorption of numbers of this magnitude by cities in the
future (particularly Dacca where 40% of the urban population resides) is
unthinkable. The Economic Report estimates that the capital cost for housing
and public services of minimal urban accommodation for 3 million additional
families would be about Tk. 4.8 billion assuming Tk. 15,000 to be the urbani-
zation cost per family. This would amount to over 3.5% of the GNP assuming
a growth rate of 5% in GNP over this period.
Unemployment
15.       The First Five-Year Plan assumes a decline in unemployment from
30% at present to 25% in 1978 with a labor force of 29.4 million. The labor
force, based on the medium population estimates, is estimated to increase
from 26.2 million in 1973 to 48 million in 1993 or an increase of about 22
million during the next two decades. Out of which about 75% is estimated to
be in the rural areas in 1993 of which 42% would be absorbed in agriculture
and the rest in non-agricultural occupations. With pressure on arable land
the possibility of large-scale absorption of labor force in the agricultural
sector is limited. The Economic Report recognizes that long-term projections
of demand for agricultural labor are uncertain. It states: "Nevertheless
from what evidence is available, it is difficult to avoid the rough judgment
that the rate at which additional labor can be efficiently utilized in
Bangladesh agriculture must be expected to decline within the next decade
or so, reaching zero by the end of the century. Substantial increase in
non-agriculture employment also seems to be difficult because of two reasons.
First the dependence of non-agricultural employment on agricultural develop-
ment. Second the present underemployment in the service sector that constitutes
a large part of non-agricultural employment. However, Bangladesh will have
to live with staggering problems of unemployment and underemployment for the
next two decades as a reduction in fertility even during the next five years
is not going to reduce supply of labor considerably." The implications of
such analyses are clear that with such a slow decline in fertility the unemploy-
ment problems would remain well beyond the present century.



AiNNEX 1
Page 6
Per Capita Income
16.       No perspective plan has so far been made to estimate the long-
term increases in per capita GDP. However, the plan assumes an increase
of 5.5% in GDP (at 1972-73 prices) during 1972-73 and 1977-78. The per
capita income is expected to increase by about 2.5% per annum -- an increase
from Tk. 580 in 1972-73 to Tk. 760 in 1977-78. However, in the long-run
any failure to reduce the rate of population growth would mean that the goal
of doubling the per capita income in the shortest possible time would recede
further. As the Economic Report observes, if Bangladesh succeeded in
improving significantly on the past and achieved a growth rate of GDP of 4%
per annum over the next thirty years, it would mean only a per capita income
of $1,100 at the end of the period with the given decline in population growth rate.
Dependency Burden
17.       About 45% of the population at present is under fifteen years
of age. This proportion would decline to less than 37 by 1993 with the
assumed decline in fertility. Obviously the impact on savings and investments
of such a decline would not be substantial in terms of requirements.

BANGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Organization of the Family Planning Program
A. Historical Background
1.        The country's family planning program started with voluntary
efforts in the early 1950s when Bangladesh was a part of Pakistan. A
nation-wide government program that started in 1960 under the Ministry of
Health as a part of health services made little headway during its first
five years. In 1965 a reorganization took the family planning program
out of the Ministry of Health and established it as an independent, single-
purpose program with its own structure. There was an East Pakistan Provin-
cial Family Planning Board, in addition to Family Planning Boards at the
districts and thanas (counties). While doctors played an important part,
particularly in sterilization, the program was one of the few in the world
to be organized outside the Ministry of Health. Another feature of the system
was the use of single-purpose female workers, known as lady family planning
visitors (LFPVs) who were trained to insert IUDs and who played a prominent
role in the Pakistani program, as did male sterilization. Use of other
methods such as pills and condoms was insignificant. The Pakistani program
also made use of part-time teams of village-level motivators, normally
consisting of a local man and a local woman.
2.        During the 1960s, several important institutional developments
occurred which had lasting effects on the program, e.g., the establishment
of the University of California Health Education Project and the Sweden-
Pakistani Family Welfare Project. Three training-cum-research institutes
(TCRIs) were established to handle the training of family planning personnel.
By 1970, these scattered administrative units formed the Directorate for
Training, Research, Evaluation and Communication (called TREC), which had
overall responsibility for the functions indicated in its title and which
had particular success in developing a capacity for designing and producing
educational materials. A Postpartum Directorate was formed to be responsible
for developing postpartum family planning services in a modest number of
urban hospitals. An Inspection Directorate with primary responsibility for
supervision of the program was also established. These three Directorates
remain in existence today and their future role and administrative organiza-
tion are part of the problem of reconstituting a program that had, for all
intents and purposes, come to a halt pending such restructuring.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
3.        Although the program of the late 1960s achieved some considerable
statistical success in recruiting acceptors (Table 1), there is at present
widespread dissatisfaction with many features of the earlier program. A
main feature of the pre-independence program, now abolished, was its
extensive reliance on substantial incentive payments to both the personnel
involved in administering the program and to acceptors. It is now widely
believed that the heavy reliance on incentive payments was an important
source of financial corruption. It also led to inflated statistics of
acceptors and lack of concern for the age characteristics of those who became
acceptors, with the result that a large proportion was believed to have been
older men and women whose reproductive years were almost past. Despite these
shortcomings, there is one ex-tremely important legacy left by the earlier
program: a high proportion of the population became aware of what family
planning is and of the possibilities for limiting fertility. This knowledge
seems quite pervasive, even in the rural areas, and provides an invaluable
base on which to build for the future. Pakistan's Fourth Five-Year Plan
(1970-75) was to bring certain changes to the program. Field services were
to be brought to an intensive level through the appointment of full-time
field workers. The approach started on a pilot basis in Tangail District in
1969. The Fourth Plan was delayed until after the election of 1970. Then
came the Civil War of 1971 which resulted in Bangladesh achieving its
independence in December, 1972. The war severely disrupted almost all
aspects of national life, including the family planning program.
B. Existing Situation
4.        The main feature in the population sector since Independence
has been the search for new organizational and administrative structures
at national, district and thana levels. The past year and a half or so
has therefore been a period of hiatus during which the Government has been
re-thinking what it ought to be doing in order to slow down population
growth and what kind of organization ought to be set up to formulate and
carry out a new program. However, following Independence the Family Planning
Board (renamed the National Family Planning Board - NFPB) along with TREC
and the Postpartum and Inspection Directorates were placed under the Ministry
of Health, which was renamed the Ministry of Health and Family Planning
(MOHFP). An interministerial board (also called NFPB) was formed in 1972
for policy-making, program guidance and coordination of activities with
other ministries (Chart 8452). So far, however, the Board has not met and
consequently has given no direction to the program. There are still many
clinics (about 400) offering some kind of family planning services, but the
level of activity has been drastically reduced since the war (Table 2).
Therefore, a considerable staff still on the family planning budget is not
contributing significantly to the national family planning program.
5.        The MDHFP proposed to extend basic health and family planning
services at the village level throughout the country by appointing two-
person teams of multipurpose workers (one man and one woman) to be designated
family welfare workers. They would be responsible for inoculations, maternal



4NNEX 2
Page 3
and child health and family planning services, sanitation, and other simple
health tasks, including record-keeping and health and family planning
education. The Ministry will begin by trying to put in place some 12,000
teams by 1978, one for every 6,000 persons (the ultimate objective is one
for every 4,000). There were on the Government payroll about 10,000 single-
purpose health workers (most of them underemployed malaria eradication and
family planning workers); these have now been redesignated and retrained as
family welfare workers for health services in rural areas. The standard
pattern of rural health services in Bangladesh is comprised of a health.
complex covering an average population of about 180,000 to 200,000 in a thana.
A "complex" consists of (a) a rural health center (RHC) with outpatient
facilities and a dispensary plus (b) an inpatient unit with six hospital beds
and (c) outpatient health subcenters, responsible to the RHC at the union
level (each thana consists of about 8 to 10 unions). The long-term goal is
to have one subcenter in each union and 100-bed hospital in each thana. But
the first plan envisages one rural health complex (with a 25-bed maternity
ward unit) and three subcenters at the union level instead of 10. The plan,
therefore, calls for the construction of (i) 196 rural health complexes to
complete the coverage of all 356 rural thanas (150 thanas already have complexes,
but with a six-bed ward, and 10 are under construction) (ii) over 1,000 health
subcenters, and (iii) 25-bed maternity ward unit at each of the RHCs in each
of the 356 thanas.
C. First Five-Year Plan Proposals
6.        The population program for the next five years as outlined in
the Five-Year Plan (1973-78) suggests various bold and unconventional
approaches. The Plan states that the first requisite of a successful pro-
gram is the realization of the gravity of population problems at all levels
of political leadership and the total commitment to its solution. The
first task, it adds, is therefore to launch a major educational and motiva-
tional campaign to bring home to the public the seriousness of the country's
population problem. Furthermore, it suggests that (a) all ministries which
maintain regular public contact (and specifically the Ministries of Local
Government, Rural Development and Cooperatives, Agriculture, Education, Labor
and Social Welfare, and Information and Broadcasting) should be required
to undertake family planning education and motivation programs of their
own, (b) all voluntary organizations involved in family planning programs
or social work must be given adequate encouragement and support, (c) the
Government should consider imposing increasingly strong disincentives against
additional children after the second child, and (e) the Government should
create a climate where the possibility of more drastic measures such as
compulsory sterilization of the spouse after the second child, legalization
of abortion and making it available free of cost, as well as social measures
for the emancipation of women, etc., may be considered.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
7.        A major problem in launching the national population planning
program has been the design of an acceptable, workable organizational
structure. The Five-Year Plan provides that while the Ministry of Health
and Family Planning will, of course, play a central role in the family planning
program (largely concerned with the delivery of services) the success of
the population program in the broader sense (changing the social attitudes
towards family size) would depend on the cooperative effort of other con-
cerned ministries. These ministries will therefore have to strengthen their
capability in order to effectively play their new roles. Their activities
will, of course, need coordination within a central strategy and program.
The Plan proposes the creation of a Population Planning Division (which
would be headed by a Secretary) but it does not state where this new division
would be located.
8.        A Population Planning Division will need more authority than can
be conferred on it by any decision, however sound, on its location within
the Government. The Division will need support and endorsement of its policy
proposals from the policy-making level of Government and it will need
support for its administrative coordinating role from senior officers in
the line ministries involved in population activities. The Five-Year Plan
proposes to deal with the first of these two problems by establishing a
National Population Council. This will be chaired by the Prime Minister
and the Minister of Health and Family Planning will serve as Vice Chairman;
other members will include the ministers of ministries with population
programs, the Deputy Chairman of the Planning Commission (the Prime Minister
is the Commission's ex-officio Chairman), the Planning Commission member
responsible for population matters, representatives of the political opposi-
tion in Parliament, and representatives of the press. The Secretary of the
proposed Population Planning Division would serve as Secretary of the Council.
The second of the two organizational problems, that of administrative
coordination, would be dealt with by creating a Central Coordination Committee
consisting mostly of the permanent secretaries of the executing ministries
(it would be chaired by the Minister of Health and Family Planning and the
Secretary of the New Population Planning Division would serve as the Vice
Chairman). However, it still leaves the problem of coordination of activities
of different ministries in the field for which the plan proposes coordination
committees at the district and thana levels. These coordination committees
would consist of field officers of ministries involved in population programs
and would be chaired by the Chairmen of the (new) District and Thana Boards
- elected bodies for the coordination of all development programs. The
proposed legal coordination committees would not be responsible directly to
the Central Coordinating Committee; however, local coordination problems
that are not resolved by the local coordination committees would reach the
Central Coordinating Committee by referral upwards through the line ministries
concerned. The organizational structure as proposed in the Plan is
reflected in Chart 8951.



IS,EX 2
Page  5
D. Emerging Organizational Structure
9.        A recent decision by the Prime Minister has put the Population
Planning Division proposed in the First Five-Year Plan under the Ministry
of Health and Family Planning (now renamed the Ministry of Health and Popu-
lation Planning - MOHPP). Consequently, the MOHPP will have two divisions
(a) the Health Division to be responsible for the delivery of integrated
health and family planning services and (b) the newly created Population
Planning Division under a full-time Secretary responsible for coordination
of program activities. In addition, the Health and Planning Section of the
Planning Commission (being renamed the Health and Population Planning Section
- IPPS) will have responsibilities related to the review of the program and
allocation of funds. Each of the other concerned ministries are developing
their capabilities by the appointment of a Population Program Officer.
Health Division
10.       The Health Division is. responsible for the facilities and personnel
through whom health services and family planning services are provided. The
way family planning personnel and supervisors fit into this structure is
diagrammed in Chart 9486. There are two main branches of facilities and
personnel: the Rural Health Complex Scheme (which reports to the Director
of hlealth Services in the Hlealth Directorate) and those facilities and
personnel which are not part of the Rural Health Scheme (these consists of
two programs, the Postpartum Program and the Training-cumn-Research Institutes).
As one reviews the system at the district and thana clinic levels and as it
concerns the village-level family welfare workers;, there is a split between
the health and family planning staff responsible for overall health and family
planning programs. For example the family planning officers are responsible
for the administration of the family planning program activities and the
technical overall supervisors, while the district technical officer and the
thana health inspectors are responsible for the work of clinic staff and
village level workers. Further, not all staff in this system are carried on
a common budget nor consider themselves part of a common service. This is
true even among different groups of welfare workers responsible to the health
inspector, who has under him staff paid from a Health budget, others paid
from a malaria eradication budget, others from a family planning budget.
These divisions within a service that has supposedly been integrated reflect
the historical facts of prior parallel (i.e., non-integrated) services,
budgets, and supervisory hierarchies. It is reported that these workers
continue to think of themselves and their accountability in terms of pre-
integration budgets and relationships. In short, integration does not exist
at the budgetarv level, where the old separate budget distinctions apparently
still exist. The Acting Project Director of the rural health complex
scheme Is still the head of the Malaria Eradlcation Program and carries his
new responsibilitv as an additional function. For effective integration the
following actions should be taken: (a) remove the budgetary distinction



ANNEX 2
Page 6
between health and family planning, (b) carefully redefine the role of
thana family planning offices to fit into the integrated set up, and (c)
appoint a full-time Director of Health Services who should be given clear
authority to administer and discipline the delivery of integrated services.
Population Planning Division
11.       The PPD has just been established and therefore has a limited staff.
It is reported that the Minister of MOHPP proposes to transfer major functions,
staff and resources (not related to the delivery of family planning services)
of the existing NFPB, TREC and Inspection Directorate to the PPD. As in the
Health Division this Division will have two levels: (a) the 'Secretariat'
that performs the functions of policy making and guidance, and (b) the
'Directorate' which are the operational cadres responsible for carrying out
specific functional programs. The internal organization of the PPD has not
yet been determined (clarification is to be a condition of credit effectiveness).
Present thinking is that there will be one Joint Secretary, two Deputy
Secretaries and three or four section officers at the Secretariat level. At
the Directorate level there will be a Director-General and a number of Directors
for different types of functions (Chart 9486).
12.       The functions and responsibilities of the PPD as proposed by the
Government are given below:
(a) Development of coordinated schemes for population activities
for all concerned ministries and their Drocessing for approval
by appropriate authorities (i.e. Planning Commission, National
Economic Council, etc.).
(b) Internal evaluation of population projects of all relevant
ministries. (External evaluation will be arranged by the
Planning Commission).
(c) Act as contact point for foreign donors in matters of
implementation of aided projects.
(d) Direct administrative respomsibility of entire Dopulation
activities except those relating to the integrated rural
health complex scheme, thie postpartum program and clinical familv
planing services which wiill be tinder the Healthl Division.
The  PPI) however, will have supervisorv control over the
population activities of the Hlealth Division.
(e)  Inspection, supervision, and coordinated implementation of
approved schemes of all relevant ministries.
(f) Assist the National Population Council in developing
national policv and act as Secretariat to the Council.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
(g) Initiate and follow up the introduction of legislative
measures relating to population policies.
(h) Conduct relevant operational research in the country to
study the effectiveness of different measures and evolve
new methodologies for population planning.
(i) Training personnel involved in population programs in
different ministries.
(j) Arrange for the production of information and education
materials and evolve suitable communication strategies.
Health and Population Planning Section (PPS) of the Planning Commission
13.       The Government proposes to strengthen the Health and Population
Planning Section of the Planning Commission. The PPS will have two units:
(a) a Planning Unit (existing) that will evidently discharge the functions
of allocation of funds, liason with external agencies and total program
review, and (b) an External Evaluation Unit responsible for conducting and
arranging external evaluation of program activities. Each division will be
headed by an assistant chief and will have appropriate staff. The PPS will
discharge the following functions and responsibilities:
(a) Review and approve proposed annual programs on population
activities prepared by different ministries and agenices;
(b) Allocate funds for the population program activities in the
country to different ministries and agencies on the basis
of review of progress of programs in consultation with the
PPD and to determine intersectoral priorities;
(c) Liase with external agencies for technical coordination of
external assistance;
(d) Assist the PPD in coordination of the population planning
efforts of ministries and agencies and in supervising the
implementation of population activities;
(e) Arrange and carry out external evaluation of the population
program of various ministries and agencies and ensure
necessary follow up measures; and
(f) Receive internal evaluation reports from the PPD for use in
preparing annual programs.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
Other Ministries
14.       None of the implementing ministries except the MOHPP has had
experience in planning and carrying out Dopulation activities. Therefore, a
population program officer (PPO) is proposed to be appointed in each of these
ministries for the purpose. There will be one PPO plus an assistant at the
Ministry of Local Government and Rural Development in addition to an adviser.
There will be three PPOs at the Ministry of Labor and Social Welfare and
two advisers. The Ministries of Agriculture, Education and Information and
Broadcasting will each have one PPO. These seven PPOs will: (a) ensure that
their own ministries carry out their section of the overall program; (b)
maintain a flexible to and fro channel of communication so that the PPD can
intervene when necessary and in return receive feedback direct from the field.
So that they may gain the experience beyond their present work necessary to
carry out their new functions, all the PPOs will be given a orientation course.
This will commence with a two-weeks orientation in Dacca (IDA will provide a
consultant to help design the course) followed by a week's visit to the thanas
where the schemes will eventually be working. They will. then go to other
countries to visit ongoing family planning programs.
National Population Council
15.       The National Population Council has not yet been formed.  But an
agreement has been reached during negotiations that the National Population
Council shall have been established, with substantially the composition and
functions as set out in the First Five-Year Plan as a condition of credit
effectiveness. The composition given in the Plan is as follows:

Central Coordination Committee
16.       While the committees at the district and thana levels are yet to
be formed, the Central Coordination Committee has been formed by the order
of the Government on November 27, 1974. The order states that the Govern-
ment "is pleased to constitute a Central Coordination Committee on popula-
tion activities with immediate effect to coordinate and help develop inter-
ministrial activities in the population program. The composition of the
Committee is as follows":
(a) Minister for Health, Population Planning, Labor and

External Assistance
1.        The Planning Commission has allocated Tk. 700 million (US$93 mil-
lion) for the population program during the First Five-Year Plan (1974-78).
The Plan's foreign exchange component was originally estimated at just
under 20%, but this proportion is rising as specific programs are taking
shape. The expenditure target is to be financed from the national budget
and from several foreign donors. It appears that external assistance
mav provide around 75% of total financing during the first three to four
years of the'Plan, with two-thirds of this assistance on a grant basis.
2.        The UNFPA project, for which an agreement has been signed for three
years, includes US$5.2 million for eight components that have been worked
out in considerable detail, plus US$4.8 million for two broad components
for which detail are to be worked out later. The eight presently-planned
components include: (a) technical assistance for census development and data
processing, (b) rural development cooperatives and population education,
(c) family planning motivation and services in industry and plantations,
(d) population education and training for labor officers and labor organizations,
(e) training program for workers in an integrated national health and family
planning program, (f) teaching various aspects of population in medical schools,
(g) strengthening the family planning clinical program with emphasis on MCH-
based family planning, and (h) organization and management of comprehensive
health and family planning supplies and delivery of service; UNICEF is concerned
with this. An additional USS4.8 million will be expended on projects now
under appraisal: (i) construction of the union level health sub-centers,
(j) communication, information and education activities, possibly for rehabil-
itating the TREC at the MOHPP, (k) population education in schools, and
(1) health education.
3.        So far the largest bilateral assistance is from the United States
Agencv for International Development (USAID), which in 1q72 and 1973 mnade
$6.5 million available for health facilities, manpower retraining, medical
equipment, and contraceptive supplies. USAID is likely to make another $8-10
million directlv available to the program over the 1974-77 period; an important
form of indlrect USAID assistance will he provided by contracts (a) with the
University of Michigan for help to TREC and (h) with Population Services Inc.
to test the feasibility of expanding contraceptive distribution through private
commercial channels. The Swedish International Development Authority (STDA)
has already committed US$1.0 million for specific population assistance in



ANNEX 3
Page 2
1974 as part of its general assistance. The U.K.'s Overseas Development
Ministry (ODM) has committed US$78,000 equivalent to the Census Commissioner's
Office, for a retrospective survey of fertility and mortality following the
national census of February, 1974.
4.        Several private agencies will also make important financial and
technical assistance contributions to the program. The Pathfinder Fund is
supporting the postpartum program and a model clinic in Dacca through a
grant of US$1.5 million. The Population Council plans to conduct one of
its world-wide Taylor Berelson postpartum demonstration projects at a total
cost of about Tk. 1.5 crores (US$2 million). The Ford Foundation's Dacca
office has recommended approval of a two-year grant of US$300,000 for support
of a new Population Studies Center (PSC) at the Bangladesh Institute of
Development Studies. The Foundation will also support a pilot project for
the development of population education curriculum, and a demographic unit
in the Planning Commission. Canada's International Development Research
Center may contribute C$ 30,000 towards applied population research. Com-
munications among donors have been relatively good and there does not appear
to be any serious danger of overlap among donor activities. The existing
Family Planning Section in the Planning Commission-has successfully coordinated
these efforts.

BANGLADESH
Draft Terms of Reference for a Management Study
Introduction
1.        Terms of reference for a management study will be elaborated, with-
in the framework described below, between the consulting firm and the Govern-
ment of Bangladesh with IDA's concurrence. The broad outlines for such a
study are described below followed by a timetable for its implementation.
Draft Terms of Reference
2.        The primary objective of the management study will be to review the
existing organization and administration of the Government's population
program in order to make appropriate recommendations for the effective inte-
gration of health and family planning services and for the implementation
and coordination of population program efforts. This integration includes
the integration of the delivery of effective services for health and family
planning, manpower training, the provision of supples, dissemination of
appropriate information, and evaluation of both health and family planning
services. To this end the study will, at the central level, review and
make recommendations on the organization and administration of family
planning services in the MOHPP and its relationship to the newly formed
Population Planning Division and its constituents, the NFPB, TREC, and the
Inspection Directorate.
3.        The study should also review and make recommendations on the
appropriate organizational relationship and optimum administrative procedure
between the central, district, thana and field levels, and also between
relevant health personnel, family planning personnel, and personnel of
other units working in population at each of these levels. Ideally, the
study should aim at reviewing and drafting or suggesting amendments as
appropriate to the functions and responsibilities of each health unit in-
sofar as its work is related to the population program and of each family
planning unit at all administrative levels; and to review and propose appro-
priate amendments to the job description of the principal family planning
workers and workers in the health field whose work is related to population.
Last, but not least, the study will review the present data information flow
and evaluation system and make recommendations to develop an efficient
system of information feedback and analysis for improving program management.
Implementation
4.        The study will be undertaken by mianagement consultants and on terms
and conditions acceptable to IDA. When the study is completed the Government

wish to do so, before any of these are implemented. The following is a

POPULATION PROJECT
Availability and Training of Health and Family Planning Manpower
1.        The family welfare visitors (FWVS) are the main category of health
personnel used for family planning services. Various categories of para-
medical personnel and their training is shown in Chart 8949. A major
expansion in facilities for training family welfare visitors (a category
of paramedical manpower) has been proposed by the Government. The main
constraint on the expansion of training is the shortage of tutors and
physical facilities. At present there are only about 400 FWVs in the
country. The Government has also decided to reduce the period of training
of FWVs from 27 months to 18 months by condensing the courses. Neither
the medical nor the paramedical students receive any field training at
present in community health or family planning. Also no arrangements exist
at present to train medical students with regard to the delivery of clinical
contraceptives. The project attempts to strengthen the training program
for health and family planning manpower by constructing, equiping and
furnishing:
(a) One college of nursing in Dacca to replace the existing
inadequate facilities. Also three micro buses and one
sedan will be provided for field training of students. The
estimated requirement of tutor nurses up to 1983 and their
availability for the project's expanded facilities are
given in Tables 1 and 2.
(b)  Eight family welfare visitor training schools to expand the
training facilities. The availability and program require-
ments of FWVs up to 1983 are shown in Tables 3, 4 and 5.
Also the project includes one micro bus and one sedan for
each school to carry the students for field training.
(c) Eight field training thana health centers along with
dormitories for 25 students to provide opportunity for
field training. Also the project includes eight micro

BANGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Model Family Planning Clinics
This annex describes the functions, administration and facilities
for the model clinics. These clinics would establish an entirely new
facility for health and family planning programs and therefore, their
operating cost for two years is included in the project.
Functions of the Clinic
1.        The major functions of the clinic will be to:
(a)  train medical students in family planning methods
with special emphasis on teaching new techniques
for abortion and sterilization;
(b)  support teaching of family planning by providing
facilities and clinical material to the medical faculty
members and medical students;
(c)  carry out clinical research and evaluation works on the
various contraceptive methods; and
(d)  increase availability of clinical services to the community,
especially for abortion and sterilization.
Administration
2.        The clinics will be headed by the departmental heads (Medical
Officers) who will be responsible for the overall administration of the clinic.
Facilities
3.        In addition to the manpower facilities described below there would
be medical equipment for each of the following units (list of equipment
available separately).
(a) Administrative Unit: The proposed staff under the project
departmental head for administration will be comprised of:



ANNEX 6
Page 2
(1) an accountant,
(ii)  a central record keeper,
-(iii)  a typist,
(iv)  a receptionist,
(v)  a sister nurse-in-charge,
(vi)  a counselor to make referrals,
(vii)  a driver and driver helper assigned to transport
patients in the ambulance, and
(viii) one peon and one night guard.
(b) Female Sterilization Unit: Staffed by one part-time surgeon,
2 surgical assistants, 2 nurses, 1 nurse's aide, 1 unit
receptionist and 1 motivator.
(i)  The surgeon will work four hours per day and perform
operations according to type, on alternate days.
(ii)  The nurses and nurse's aide will work full-time.
(iii)  One motivator for follow-up.
(c) Treatment Unit: This unit will cover cases of incomplete
abortion and miscarriages and would be staffed by 1 part-time
female physician, 1 nurse and 1 unit receptionist.
(i)  The physician will divide her time between abortion
patients, contraceptive patients, and other clinic
duties as needed.
(ii)  The nurse will work full-time, dividing her time between
abortion cases, the recovery room and other clinic
duties as needed.
(d) Contraceptive Unit: Staffed by 1 female physician,
1 nurse, 1 nurse's aide, 1 unit receptionist, and
2 motivators.
Mi) The physician will work part-time to examine
patients for giving pills and to insert IUDs;



ANNEX 6
Page 3
(ii) The nurse and nurse's aide will work full-time;
and
(iii) two motivators for follow-up.
(e) Vasectomy Unit: Staffed by 4 part-time surgeons, 2 nurses,
1 nurse s aide, and 1 unit receptionist.
(i)  The surgeons will work four hours per day performing
vasectomy operations;
(ii) The nurses and nurse's aide will work full-time and
will be in charge of the vasectomy recovery room.
Reporting and Evaluation
3.        A report on clinic performance will be submitted to the concerned
Joint-Secretary, MOHPP each month with a copy to the Inspection Directorate.
The report will contain:
(a) progress in clinic performances under each clinical
unit and service unit;
(b)  training given and problems encountered;
(c)  the nmber of new patients and follow-up patients
per month for each unit;
(d) age and parity of new patients in each unit;
(e)  training received in-house (who was trained and who
directed the training); and
(f) the type and cause of each case of complication and
the results of treatment.
Facilities
4.        The project provides for the required equipment and recurring
cost of the clinics for two years assuming that the work would be initiated
while the clinics are under construction. The details are given in the
table at the end of this annex.

BANGLADESII
POPULATION PROJECT
Functions and Training of Female Family Welfare Workers
1.        The Ministry of Health and Population Planning (MOHPP) has decided
to extend health and family planning services through teams of multinurpose
workers throughout the country.  Each team is comprised of one male and one
female worker.  These workers, to be called family welfare workers, would be
responsible for inoculations, sanitation, maternal and child health and family
planning, record keeping and simple health care. The ultimate objective of
one team per 4,000 population (requiring 19,000 teams or 38,000 workers) is
too expensive for the country to afford at this stage. Therefore, the MO?PP
has decided to have one team per 6,000 population or about 12,000 teams by
the end of the five-year period. About 10,000 existing family planning and
other health workers (all males except 500) have been retrained as multi-
purpose workers. Thus there are about 10-11,000 female workers to be re-
cruited to complete the teams. Without the female counterparts, the maternal
and child health and family planning services, as well, as accompanying
education and motivation programs, will suffer. The males at present have
heavy workloads and cannot possible do justice to simple health care, much
less to MOHPP. They are expected to cover some 30-40 households per day to
complete the first round of contracts and records on eight different forms
in a month. The mission suggests recruitment of 7,500 female familv workers
(FFlW's) in the five-year plan period (Table 1).  The project includes salary
support and training of 3,700 such workers.  Another 1,800 FFWNT will he pro-
vided by the UNFPA.  It is recommended that the FFWls have at least eight
years of schooling and be residents of the same village or thana in which they
will work. They should be given three months of training at the district
level in groups of 30. About 100 FFhWs can be trained in each district in
a year. The district medical officers and 3-4 staff assigned to district
headouarters should be the trainers.
2.        The functions of the FFTW should be to:  (a) motivate the eligible
women through individual talks and group teaching, (b) provide information
regarding availability of contraceptive services, (c) deliver certain tvpes
of contraceptives, particularly pills (they will be given the list of con-
traindications and training in examining the patient for these), (d) refer
clients to health centers and mobile clinic teams for IUD insertions and
sterilization operations (e) follow-up acceptors, and (f) identify pregnant
women and newborn babies for MCH services and for referral to health centers.
The work of the FFWWs should be supervised technically by FWVs to be posted



ANNEX 7
Page 2
at the union level sometime in the future: The administrative supervision
is to be performed by a health inspector who is supported by one assistant
inspector per union; for every six assistant inspectors there would be one
inspector. In other words, in each thana there would be eight to twelve
assistant inspectors and two inspectors to supervise the work of the FFWWs.
The project provides for training (including field material) of these FFWWs
and salary support for four years (details are given in the table at the
end of the annex). In addition, in nineteen thanas special motivation kits
will be provided on a pilot basis (see Annex 14).
3.        The FFWWs would be appointed and trained in phases.  It is suggested
that out of 3,700 FFWWs provided in the project first, 1,500 should be
appointed by December 1975, another 1,500 by March 1976 and the remaining 700
by June 1976.
4.        The estimated costs of salary support and training of FFWW are
given at the end of the annex.
