BAUNGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Service Statistics System
1.        This annex examines the system of collection of vital statistics
and data on acceptors (service statistics) that is being planned and to some
extent initiated by the MOHPP. It further suggests the required modifica-
tion in the forms, system of reporting, analysis and feedback of informa-
tion.
I. Ongoing Program
2.        The Ministry of Health and Population Planning has been developing
a service statistics system for the integrated health and family planning
program with a view toward obtaining the data required to monitor the program.
To supplement this, the Ministry also has plans to undertake assessment of
family planning service facilities and studies of other operational prob-
lems. The responsibility for the implementation of service statistics
system has been entrusted to the Inspection Directorate (ID) of the Minis-
try.
3.        As of now, there has been only partial development of the basic
elements of the service statistics system, namely, (a) records of activities
generated at different levels of the program organization during the course
of its implementation, and the immediate results achieved; (b) reports on
these activities and results sent to higher levels of program administration
which monitor the program; (c) processing and feedback of service statistics;
and (d) facilities for implementation of the system. Health cards and couple
registration cards/forms were provided for the entire population of the
country. They are being updated during the course of return visits of workers
to households/families in connection with health and family planning work.
These cards and forms constitute potential sources of data for evaluation
of performance in the fields of health as well as family planning. Forms
are developed for reporting of daily performance of family welfare workers
(FWWs), and also for weekly consolidation of these reports by the assistant
inspector or inspector at the thana health center. Channels for reports
from the level of thana health center to the ID, and procedures for consoli-
dation of reports at each stage are yet to be decided. Neither record nor
report forms have so far been developed for use by other service units, such
as government medical institutions, and family planning clinics under non-
governmental agencies, prescribing only clinical or all contraceptive methods.



YNNEX X7
Page 2
4.        The technical staff at various levels higher than assistant inspec-
tor for the implementation of the system includes one statistical assistant
at district office, and one officer on special duty (OSD) of the rank of
Deputy Director and one Deputy Director (DD) at the ID and one statistician
of the family planning board. Both senior positions of Deputy Director rank
are at present vacant. The other staff of the ID are presently engaged
mostly in spot-inspection of field work. No comprehensive service statistics
system as such has been set in operation. Also, facilities are not available
at the ID to cope with the data processing requirements of the system when
service statistics from all areas/units commence flowing regularly. The
project includes assistance for the establishment and efficient operation of
the service statistics system in so far as it relates to family planning
program only.
II. Family Planning Service Statistics System
5.        Objective:  To establish an efficient system for routine collec-
tion, collation, analysis and feedback of adequate information on various
kinds of efforts made within the family planning program, and results that
are immediately achieved in terms of acceptance and use of the program
methods of contraception, in order to help monitor the national program at
various levels of program administration, such as thana, district and national
levels.
6.        Contents:  All service units, such as FWWs and medical and other
institutions presenting contraceptive methods, will maintain suitable fam-
ily planning client records, and prepare separate forms denoting important
characteristics of new acceptors. FWWs will continue to send daily reports
as at present, and two sets of acceptors' characteristics (AC) forms to the
assistant inspector. He will consolidate the daily reports of all FWWs under
his supervision into monthly report for each sub-union, and send one set of
reports to the thana health center and another set of reports together with
a set of AC forms directly to the ID at Dacca. Similar monthly reports and AC
form will be sent regularly every month by all other service units to the4r
respective immediately higher supervisory level, namely, the health ce ter
or district health and family planning office, and also simultaneouslv tc  t'
ID directly. Important entries made on the AC forms will be verified in
the field by assistant inspectors or the staff of the thana health centcr
and district office. Based on reports of assistant inspectors for sub-unions
and of other service units in the thana area, the thana health center will
prepare a consolidated monthly report for each union and send copies of the
reports to the District Office, where they will be consolidated by thana.
Copies of these reports will be sent to the regional office and to the ID.
All monthly reports will indicate progress in rural and urban areas sepa-
rately. Thus, the ID will receive monthly reports together with AC forms
directly from the periphery, and consolidated monthly reports for the thana,
from districts. It will also receive detailed progress reports from thanas
and districts on the staff in position together with the status of their
training, in their respective areas; and from training centers, the number



dage .i
of Ssa-s of various categories thiat are given -n-serv 7 ce trainirg.  The
ID will collate and analyze the informiation received, andi present the data
in a suitable form to the senior executives for their information regarding
policy decisions. It will also feed suitable data to the peripheral levels
to help them assess their own performance as compared to others.
7.        Administration:  The Director of ID will be in charge of overall
guidance to the project. When the OSD and DD are appointed in the currently
vacant posts, one of them will be in charge of the establishment of the
system and routine tabulations, and the other in charge of a detailed anal-
ysis of data received in the reports and special studies of data from the
records in the field. Both of them will go on observation tours for three
months to countries in Asia where service statistics systems are well devel-
oped. Provision is made for additional staff at the ID, one statistician at
district office (in addition to the existing post of one statistical assistant)
and one statistical assistant at each thana health center. The OSD and DD
will train the staff of the ID and the statisticians at the districts for a
period of two weeks in Dacca. The district statisticians, with assistance
from the ID, will train the statistical assistants at thana health centers
in their respective districts for a period of two weeks at district head-
quarters. An adviser will help in the development of forms for use in the
system, programs for tabulation of data in the reports received, and analy-
tical studies of data in the records and reports of the system, and training
programs. The terms of reference for the adviser are given in Appendix I.
8.        Coverage:  The system covers the entire family planning program
of the Ministry of Health and Population Planning as well as of other min-
istries. It will also include in its purview commercial distribution of
contraceptives when sponsored by the Government.
9.        Staff and Other Requirements:  The project includes the following
additions:
a.   Personnel
(i)  Two assistant directors (statistics) 4 statisticians
-- these together with the statistician currently in
position -- will supervise operation of the system
in the four regions of the country, and undertake
routine tabulations and analysis of data, and from
the regions, undertake studies based on data in the
records;
(ii)  20 statistical assistants to scrutinize monthly
and other reports, code and collate data in the
reports and collect and code data from field
records.



_ANEX _1 7
Page 4
(iii)  One office superintendent to assist in the general
administration of the entire unit in the ID and in
the statistical units in districts and in the thana
health center.
(iv) Four steno-typists to work for the 12 senior officials
including the OSD, DD and the statistician to be ap-
pointed in the existing posts, and the additional 5
assistant directors and 4 statisticians.
(v)  Two clerks for typing and maintenance of office files.
(vi) Two drivers for the two 4-wheel drive vehicles pro-
vided in the project.
(vii) Two peons to attend to the officials of the unit.
(viii) 19 Statisticians, one in each district to train
statistical assistants at thana health centers,
scrutinize monthly reports, and report to higher
levels, and to assist the ID in the collection of
data from records at the peripheral level.
b.   Vehicles and Equipment:
(i)  Two 4-wheel drive vehicles to the ID for use in
the supervision of operations at the field level,
and in the collection of data from the field records
for special analysis.
(ii) Six automatic key punching-cum-verifying machines,
1 reproducer, 1 sorter, 1 tabulator, 4 air condi-
tioners.
(iii) 34 hand calculators (15 for the ID, and one for each
district); and
(iv) Six typewriters for the ID.
c.   Operating Costs:  Provision is made for maintenance and
running of vehicles, maintenance of data processing and
calculators, data processing and office supplies, printing
of forms for family planning records, reports, and service
statistics manuals, training materials and in-service
training, special studies based on data available in
field records, and publication of important reports.
10.       Expected Results:  It is expected that adequate data will be
routinely available to administrators to monitor the program and on which
to base policy decisions. Analysis of service statistics will also help
in the identification of operational problems for studies to be undertaken
by appropriate agencies.

Z,o ,Paluation: The evaluation of the service statistics system
consists of assessing its effectiveness gauged in terms of (a) completeness
of reports received in the ID; (b) punctuality and regularity of routine
periodical tabulations; (c) usefulness of the analytical studies made of
the data of the system; and (d) most importantly, the extent to which the
findings from analyses are taken into consideration by the senior executives
while making policy decisions.

at the end of this annex.

Appendix I
Suggested Terms of Reference for the Evaluation
Adviser for the Inspection Directorate
1.        Assist the Director, Inspection Directorate in:
(a) Development of forms for records and reports, and manuals
for the family planning service statistics system.
(b) Training programs for the staff at the ID, districts
and thana health centers.
(c) Programs for processing of service statistics and feedback.
(d) Scrutiny of data on births, deaths and use of contraceptive
methods that are being collected via the family planning
and health cards/forms.
(e) Analysis of data in the reports received in the ID; and
(f) Review of the working of the service statistics systems as
a whole.
2.        Qualifications:  An expert in the analysis of family planning and
vital statistics with experience in the development of a service statistics
system for the family planning program.



1,SmAYI'7E 1 8
3BNGL20ESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Research
1.        The Planning Commission has already set up a Working Group on
the development of population research in Bangladesh. The working group
has recommended areas of priority research, listed the institutions that
have potential for conducting such research and provided the guidelines for
establishing a Population Studies Center (PSC) at the Bangladesh Institute
of Development Studies (BIDS). The Ford Foundation and the Population Coun-
cil are assisting the BIDS in setting up the PSC.
Study on the Determinants of Rep2roductive Behavior
2.        The list of research studies that has been prepared is quite com-
prehensive and puts a study of the determinants of reproductive behavior in
the country as one of the priorities. The importance of such a study, at
this stage in Bangladesh when it is launching a broad multisectoral popula-
tion program, cannot be over-emphasized. For example, the population program
for the first time gives support to the development programs of various min-
istries that are aimed at changing the role and status of women. These pilot
schemes included in the project are based on hypotheses generated from a num-
ber of surveys undertaken in the country and outside whlich showed a high nega-
tive correlation between the educational and economic status of women and fer-
tility levels. The surveys have been mainly in the nature of opinion surveys
of knowledge, attitude and practice of family planning or on the fertility
differential by socio-economic status. No in-depth analysis has so far been
made of the factors that determine the demand for a particular number of
children. The basic issue in this regard is to what extent family-size con-
siderations are based on rational economic caluclations? W4hat are var_ous
non-pecuniary considerations that determine the size of family among various
socio-economic groups and how far can their respective roles be measured
(Appendix I). An assessment, therefore, of family size considerations I'y
these factors would reveal interesting results for policy-makers. 1/
1/ T. Paul Schultz, Determinants of Fertility: A Micro Economic Model of
Choice, University of Minnesota, October, 1973. Also, see by the same
author - "Explanation of Birth Rate Changes of Space and Time; A Study
of Taiwan, "Journal of Political Economy. March/April 1973. Also, "A
Preliminary Survey of Economic Analysis of Fertility", The American
Economic Review, May 19?3.



2
Few -ivypo thescs
3.        In the search for determinants of fertility one could identify a
variety of social factors that lead Bangladesh couples to have an average
of 5 to 6 children. For example, by age 19 almost all females are married
(50% are married by the age of 13). Because their husbands are on the aver-
age 10 years older, the incidence of widowhood is according'ly high (approxi-
mately 45% of women 45-54 years old are widows). This could well intensify
the demand of mothers for children, especially sons, for security during
the period of widowhood. We observe also that female participation in the
labor force in Bangladesh has little demographic significance since only
6 percent of the women who were married were reported in the labor force in
1969. The question as it bears on fertility is not only of women participating
in paid employment, but of female opportunities generally and their status in
the family and society. This relates to the larger consideration that in a
society, where mobility is very low and traditional agriculture provides the
main source of livelihood, individual decisions and behavior are controlled
by social forces that transcend individual personalities. It is doubtful that
fertility-related policies, however, theoretically valid and administratively
efficient they may be, could achieve their potential objectives without con-
sidering community characteristics and social structure.
4.        Finally, one could advance the view that without a sense of sharing
the fruits of national development individuals are unlikely to respond
positively to social programs aimed at altering their behavior. For this
reason consideration in research needs to be given to factors that deter-
mine income distribution, social mobility and to the broad question of
equity.
5.        Many of the relationships mentioned above are not adequately
understood either in theory or in fact. Most of the needed information
will be obtained by means of field surveys. Conducting adequate and
reliable surveys requires not only technical skills but also experienced
personnel and complex administrative machinery. It seems essential that
a viable research program should start on a pilot basis. Initial pilot
studies should serve as a vehicle to build personnel and organizational
capabilities and refined methodology. It should also address itself to
substantive questions.
6.        A final goal in the design is to develop a national sample frame
that serves as a base for a periodical information gathering system to
monitor and understand fertility behavior and its social and economic de-
terminants. The trade-off in terms of the cost and benefits of the quality
and utility of needed information between a national-community oriented
sample design and selected area design will be given careful consideration.
The constraints of personnel availability and supervision are only some of
the considerations. Others are the speed of the gathering of information,
its analysis and utilization.



Page 3
Mthodo}c
7.        An initial part of this study will be to review and build on exist-
ing demographic and socio-economic knowledge of Bangladesh and utilize find-
ings of current research on the process of social change in Bangladesh, now
in progress by the investigators and others at Johns Hopkins University.
8.        The second part of the study is to blend existing theory and knowl-
edge in the context of Bangladesh's current experience in order to develop
a program of studies that is both relevant and feasible. Studies will be
designed to focus on the following two basic dimensions of fertility behav-
ior:
(a) Age at marriage and marital stability;
(b) Family formation: completed family size and child
spacing.
To understand these two basic dimensions of fertility behavior the following
determinants of fertility that are subject to direct and/or indirect govern-
ment policy instruments will be examined:
(a) Child value and productivity of child labor (including
the interaction with education, infant and child
mortality and morbidity);
(b) Female employment and work opportunities;
(c) Family structure and networks; and
(d) Income distribution, equity and social mobility.
9.        While these factors are subject to direct and/or indirect policy
intervention, there is a gap in knowledge about the factors that influence
the effectiveness of various policy actions. Therefore attention will be
given to examining, on individual and community levels, variables related
to the implementation and effectiveness of social policies. An important
question in this regard is the reception accorded the instruments and agen-
cies of policy and conversely, the view of their task taken by those respon-
sible for policy implementation.
Phasing
10.       These studies are considered as a three-phase operation.  An im-
portant output of Phase I will, be a detailed development of plans for Phase II
and Phase III with provisional estimates of budget, time schedules and needs
for personnel and institutional development. Phase II includes the develop-
ment in detail of pilot studies that would have as an important objective
the development of research and technical capabilities in Bangladesh in
preparation for more substantial research projects that constitute the main



Pae4
c-iv'my    aThase II.  Phase 1l7  is concerned with analyss , interpretation
and evaluation of findings, their policy implications and projections of
plans for future research activities. Work on the Phase I was completed on
October 15, 1974 through Bank assistance.
11.       A major part of the work in these studies will be done in Bangladesh
in consultation with local professionals and with the services of some outside
consultants. It is also expected that Johns Hopkins University, which was
involved in Phase I of the program and has experts either from Bangladesh or
with extensive experience, will participate actively in the work.
Work Schedule
Completion of detailed planning
of studies                         July 1975
Initiation of pilot studies          August 1975
Completion of pilot studies          December 1976
Initiation of nation mid studies   January 1977
Completion of nation mid studies   June 1978
Cost
12.       The total cost of the research program is estimated at US$420,500.
The details are given in the table at the end of this annex.
Study of National Laws for Their Pronatality Impact
13.       It is not known what effect, if any, existing national laws may
have on the size of families couples choose to have. An examination of
national laws for their pronatality impact might reveal desirable or un-
desirable biases in the country's law which should be strengthened or
changed. It is therefore recommended that the PPD should establish a
committee that will commission a study of national laws. The study might
include such topics as tax law, property law (including the law of inheri-
tance), family law (including the lawful age at marriage), criminal law
(including e.g. law of abortion) and similar subjects. The committee would
submit to IDA the recommendations and or changes in laws as appear appropri-
ate. The terms of reference for this study should be acceptable to IDA.
Work Schedule
Formation of the Committee                March 1975
Submission of terms of reference
to IDA                                  April 1975
Completion of the study                   June 1976
Submission of recommendations
to NPC                                  December 1976

BANGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Project Administration
1.        This annex describes the agencies responsible for the overall pop-
ulation project administration and the structure within each of the six min-
istries responsible for the implementation of the different components of
the project. The organizational structure is depicted in Chart 9488.
I. Overall Administration
2.        Project administration will rely primarily on those existing agen-
cies within the Government that will be responsible for the population plan-
ning program administration and implementation (Annex 2).  No new entity
for project administration will be created except a Building Planning and
Design Unit (BPDU) which is to be established at the MOHPP. The BPDU will
have major responsibility for the implementation of the construction compo-
nent of the project. All non-construction activities will be the responsi-
bility of the respective executing ministries or agencies, which shall main-
tain progress reports and conduct internal evaluation. The newly established
Population Program Division (PPD), in collaboration with the Health and
Population Planning Section of the Planning Commission (PPS), will have major
responsibility for overall monitoring, evaluation and coordination of the
project's activities. A project financial officer will be placed in the PPD.
The office will have adequate staff to check the use of resources in the
field. Also, on behalf of all donors, a population project officer has been
proposed for the Bank's resident mission in Dacca to provide continuous
project follow up.
Population Planning Division
3.        The Population Planning Division will be responsible for the fol-
lowing:
(a)  To supervise the implementation of project activities
of the different ministries;
(b) To provide technical support to the different minis-
tries and agencies involved in the planning and imple-
mentation of their schemes in the project;
(c) To conduct and arrange for training of the personnel
involved in the schemes of different ministries in the
project;



aEmi3x 20
PaSge 2
(d) To assist in the Internal evaluation of the programs
of the different ministries and in the analysis of
the results of internal evaluation and its feedback
to the Planning Commission;
(e)  To coordinate and insure timely procurement of serv-
ices, vehicles and equipment for the project; and
(f) To coordinate the statements of expenditures and
the signing of withdrawal applications through the
project's financial officer.
4.        The PPD is expected to have adequate staff to discharge the func-
tions described above. However, for purposes of centralizing project finan-
cial responsibility and preparing applications for withdrawal of the credit
proceeds, a special project finance officer shall be designated by the Min-
istry of Finance to be placed at the PPD. This officer will have adequate
staff in the General Accounts Office to check the use of personnel, equip-
ment, vehicles and other necessary resources in the field before signing the
withdrawal application.
Health and Population Planning Section of the Planning Commission (PPS)
5.        The PPS will have the following responsibilities:
(a) To review and approve the schemes prepared by the
different ministries and agencies;
(b) To allocate funds for these schemes of different
ministries and agencies on the basis of the progress
review;
(c) To liaise with IDA and other donors and provide
regular progress reports;
(d) To arrange for implementing external evaluations of
the project activities under different ministries
and agencies and to ensure necessary follow-up
measures; and
(e) To receive internal evaluation reports from the
Population Planning Division for use in preparing
the report for proper feedback to different agencies
involved in the project.
b.        The project provides for the establishment of an external evalua-
tion unit at the PPS (see Annex 15). Although the PPS already has a small
staff and some resources it will get the necessary technical support from
outside to form two study groups on (a) information, education and commu-
nication, and (b) evaluation and research activities. These groups are



e ct.d t  p=ov de tecemicai advice in t'.se tw  areas to the FPS in order
to plan and review the program of the different ministries. The functions
and composition of these groups are given in Appendices I and II.
Population Project Officer for the Bank Resident MIssion
7.        Because of the size and complexity of the project, IDA and other
donors feel that there is need for continuous monitoring and follow-up.
Therefore, as indicated earlier, a proposal has been made to place a popu-
lation project officer in the Bank resident mission in Dacca. The cost of
such an appointment for four years is to be shared by IDA and other donors
on a pro rata basis.
II. Implementation of Specific Components
Non-Health Ministries
8.        To provide a locus of project activities population program offi-
cers (PPOs) are to be appointed in all ministries (except the HOHPP) involved
in the implementation of the project, namely Ministries of Local Government,
Rural Development and Cooperatives, Agriculture, Education, Information and
Broadcasting, Labor and Social Welfare. The PPOs will be mainly responsible
for the administration of the project within the existing organization of
each ministry. These PPOs would report to the heads of relevant sections
(directly related to the population program) of the concerned ministries.
The PPOs would submit periodic progress reports on the implementation of
the project to the Population Planning Division through the heads of the
relevant sections. The functions of PPOs and their status in the ministry
concerned are discussed in the annexes dealing with the programs of these
ministries.
Ministry of Health and Population Planning
9.        The Health Division of the Ministry of Health and Population Plan-
ning (MOHPP), along with the Building Directorate, will be responsible for
the implementation of the total construction component of the project. In
addition, the implementation of non-construction activities under the Health
Division will be the responsibility of the Secretary responsible for family
planning program. For the construction program, the GOB will establish
within the MOHPP a Building Planning and Design Unit (BPDU).
Building Planning and Design Unit
10.       The BPDU will report directly to the Secretary.  It will have
powers to enter into contracts with consultants and suppliers of furniture
and equipment, and it will work closely with other agencies involved in the
implementation process, such as the MOHMP Planning Cell and the Building
Directorate.



M: 20
?age 4
Ii.      eTh  d-uties of the B2DU will be primarily:
(a)  to be responsible for final site selection, based
on criteria approved by IDA;
(b) to promote expeditious site acquisitions by the
local and central authorities involved;
(c) to be responsible for developing a construction
program, including planning of procurement, in
consultation with the Building Directorate;
(d) to be responsible for the establishment of standards,
preparing of functional programs, designs, and tender
documents which will be approved by and handed over
to the Building Directorate;
(e) to cooperate with the Building Directorate in tendering
procedures and supervision of civil works;
(f) to promote and coordinate timely commissioning of the
project facilities (staffing, equipment, supplies,
connecting utilities, etc.);
(g) to be responsible for preparation of the necessary
documents for procurement of furniture and equipment,
and for tendering procedures and contracts regarding
these items; and
(h) to report project progress to the Planning Commission
and to IDA on an agreed regular basis.
12.       The BPDU will be staffed with the following:
(a) a head of the unit: This will be a full-time position,
neld by either a senior civil servant or a suitable
architect or engineer. IDA should approve the creden-
tials of the candidate before his appointment, and the
salary be charged to the credit;
(b) a project architect;
(c) a senior engineer;
(d)  a project accountant (to be seconded from the MHDPP);
(e) a procurement, equipment and supplies officer with
sufficient previous experience in this area; and
(f) secretarial support to be provided by the MOHPP.



AN'.EEX 20
Page 5
-1r:ii2  Prc-ct Manage'ent Consultants
13.       To enable the BPDU to perform its duties, it should be supported
by foreign or local consultants, assisting in all its duties as mentioned
above. This will require one or more consultant firms experienced in site
selection, land acquisition procedures, construction planning and manage-
ment, equipment specification and listing, cost strategy and control, and
procedures of procurement. These services will be delivered preferably by
a foreign project management consultant in joint venture with a local firm,
since this profession became represented in Bangladesh only recently.
14.       The project management consultant will be allowed direct contact
with the MOHPP Permanent Secretary, and also access to all relevant docu-
ments regarding the project.
1.       Terms, conditions and appointment of consultants and architects
shall be mutually acceptable to the Government and IDA.
Executive Architects
16.       The project management consultant will be required to retain the
services of one or more executive architects with customary engineering and
allied technical support, responsible for detailed design and scheduling,
as well as for the preparation of tender documents and supervision of the
construction of individual project facilities by the customary periodic
inspection of work in progress. Day-to-day supervision of construction
will be provided by the field staff of the Building Directorate.
Building Utilization Studies
17.       There are three topics affecting the use and life of the physical
facilities in the program which give cause for concern. These are (a) the
weakness of building maintenance activities, which threatens to shorten
building life, (b) the weakness of procedures for procuring and distri-
buting essential supplies which, when shortages occur, makes it impossible
to offer services for which buildings are designed, and (c) uncertainty as
to the suitability of facilities until tested by use. These topics will be
the subject of studies at appropriate periods during the project life. The
proposed BPDU in the MOHPP will be responsible for supervising a study of
present and recommended supply procedures which are closely related to tile
Unit's responsibility for commissioning project buildings. This study shall
be started during the first year of the project. The BPDU will also be re-
sponsible for supervising a study of the use made of each type of project
facility and of the strengths and weaknesses of facility design. The study
will be conducted by a qualified contractor to be selected in an agreement be-
tween the Borrower and IDA; the study will be done after project facilities
have been completed and in operation for a reasonable time. The detailed
terms of reference are given in Appendix I and the cost estimates in Annex
22. The possibility of improved maintenance will be discussed with the
responsible Ministries (MOHPP and MOW).

POPULATION PROJECT
Terms of Reference of Information, Education
and Communication (IEC) Planning Working Group
The IEC Planning Working Group should consist of a core of qualified
IEC experts in the country - representatives of various ministries involved
in population programs. This group should have the following functions:
(a) preparation of an inventory of existing resources -
human, technical and institutional - existing policies
and planning procedures, and previous experiences to
identify areas of cooperation and coordination;
(b) identification and recommendation of planning approaches
and the practical ways and means to create and effectively
use a common pool of resources and technical skills;
(c) development of policies and methodology to ensure the
development of integrated "total development" communica-
tion messages within which family planning messages will
be firmly but sensitively included;
(d) devising of plan procedures to ensure the vital element
of coordination at research, inception, plan formulation
and development, implementation, field level evaluation
and feedback levels of the communication program;
(e) development of plans for evaluation of IEC activities; and
(f) identification of areas and institutes for communication
research.

Page I
BANGIADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Terms of Reference for the Evaluation Working Group
The Evaluation Working Grcup should consist of experts in the
country (from universities, research institutes, and governmental organi-
zations) to help the Population Planning Division in:
(a) preparing a detailed plan of evaluation of the
program and its various activities;
(b) assigning responsibilities for the evaluation
studies to different universities and research
institutions;
(c) discussing the methodology and framework of
evaluation studies;
(d) reviewing the system of routine program evaluation
in the country; and
(e) establishing the program information feedback system
to assist in improving program strategy and imple-
mentation.



A i'EX 20
Appendix III
Page 1
BANGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Terms of Reference Lor the Building Utilization Study
1.        The Government of Bangladesh and IDA have agreed that it would be
desirable to conduct a functional evaluation of a sample of project facili-
ties after they have been in operation for some time. This evaluation would
be important for the design of facilities as part of the subsequent expan-
sion of the rural health system. The main goal of the evaluation study is to
relate both capital costs and operational costs (salaries, materials, main-
tenance and replacements) to: (a) staff member and work-time; (b) facilities
used for various functions; and (c) the amount of services delivered (health
care, family planning, health education, in-service training, etc.).
2.        The study will also assess whether the facilities provided do in
practice constitute optimal tools for the functions to be performed within
them.
3.        The design of the study including the sample design, questionnaires,
checklists and timetable, would require expertise in architecture and health
planning on the utilization of facilities. The end-product would be a report
on the utilization of facilities containing a statistical analysis accompa-
nied by narrative commentary and a final evaluation and recommendations.
4.        The field work would be carried out by a team comprised of 2 archi-
tect/health planners, one financial analyst (or statistician with experience
in budgeting and accounting) and one public health specialist (preferably
with experience in community health 4nd family planning programs).
5.        The statistical information would need to be gathered at various
administrative levels. Other information would be obtained through inter-
views with local staff and actual observation of activities.
6.        The study will require careful preparation both by the Government
and the Bank and would be performed by consultants with thorough experience
in planning and evaluation of health facilities. The consultants would be
appointed and briefed by the Government with Bank assistance and approval
concerning their qualifications. Credit funds have been provided for this
study.



AlqNEY 20
Appendix III
Page 2
7.       Specific questions to be Included in the terms of reference of
the consultants shall be agreed upon by the Goverrment and IDA and will be
further discussed with the consultants at the time of their appointment.
Consultants will be selected by the Goverment and approved by IDA by
end of 1977 and will be appointed to initiate the study not later than
June 30, 1978.

BANGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Project Financing
1.        This annex describes the financial plan of the project and the
arrangements between various donors for the disbursement of funds.
I. Financing Plan
2.        IDA and six other donors are expected to participate in financing
the project. All donors except the Government of Australia and the Swedish
international Development Authority (SIDA) have expressed interest in fi-
nancing certain identifiable project components (parallel financing). The
Government of Australia will enter into joint financing arrangements with
IDA, and SIDA with the Government of Bangladesh. While the detailed financ-
ing plan is given in Appendix I, the amounts pledged and the type of
financing by individual donor are Riven below:
Local                Type of
Currency             Financing
(000)
Government of Australia                  A$2,000           Joint with IDA
CIDA                                    CN$2,000               Parallel
Federal Republic of Germany             DM$15,000                 "
NORAD                                   NKr 45,000                "
SIDA                                    SKr 15,000         Joint with GOB
ODM (United Kingdom)                    1 1,400                Parallel



Page v
II. Proposed Arrangement for Receipt and Disbursement of Donors Funds
3.        Some donors have expressed interest in having IDA coordinate, or
otherwise aid, in the disbursement of their funds, as well as provide super-
vision for the project. IDA's view is that such coordination is desirable,
and IDA is willing to offer its assistance in this regard consistent with
its own staff constraints. To the extent other donors wish to avail them-
selves of IDA's assistance in the disbursement process, IDA wii. administer
a Trust Fund consisting of such donor's contributions to the project.  In 
the case of parallel participation in the project, the donor would specify
the purpose for which these funds were to be spent, and IDA would ensure
that the funds were so spent. If a donor would select a joint participation,
IDA would at its discretion use the funds for the purpose of carrying out
the project. For this purpose each participating donor would open a bank
account in its Central Bank in the name of IDA and in the currency of the
donor. IDA would be entitled to make withdrawals by cable, draft, etc.
If IDA already has a Trust Fund account in a particular Central Bank, the
contribution would be deposited in that account and treated by IDA in its
records as a sub-account. A donor may elect to deposit its contribution
(i) in the form of an annual or more frequent lump sums; or (ii) at intervals
in amounts requested by IDA to meet requirements.

&ANGULDESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Demographic Impact of the Project
1.        This annex examines the number of acceptors needed to avert the
required number of births for achieving the fertility reduction as laid
down in the First Five-Year Plan of Bangladesh. It further shows (a) the
minimu number of acceptors that the program including the project can
recruit, and (b) the maximum number that can possily be recruited if the
program administration and facilities substantially improve mn the basis
of project experience. The latter reveals the extent to which the program
must be strengthened is the future mainly through additional resources to
achieve the plan objectives.
2.        Plan ObJectives:  The plan objectives implies achievement of a
net reproduction rate of 1 (two-child family) by the end of the century in
order to achieve a stationary population sometime within the next century.
The short-term goal of the plan is to reduce the birth rate from 47 per
thousand estimated at present to 43 in 1978. With the death rate declining
from 17 to 15 during the same,period, the rate of population growth is ex-
pected to decline to 2.8% from the present rate of 3.0% per annum.
Demographic Implications of Achievement of Plan Objectives
3.        It is estimated that if the Government, in fact, succeeds in
achieving its objectives the rate of growth of population would decline by
half (1.4%) by the turn of the century and the population would stabilize
at close to 200 million sometime in the second half of the next century.
The crude birth rate would be reduced to 22.3 in 1998 as compared to 47 at
present. The results of these projections are summarized in Table 1 at the
end of this annex.
4.        The above projections are based on the assumption of a decline
in total fertility from over six at present to 2.2 by the end of the cen-
tury. The rate of decline in total fertility is assumed to be slow at the
initial stages; accelerating in the latter part of the seventies and in the
eighties and then slowing down again due to the usual plateau effect in the
decline in fertility observed in many countries 1/. It is assumed that the
1/   The following are the rates of decline in fertility assumed for the
projections.

-000   -15   -0.25  -0.40  -0.55  -0.60  -0.40  -0.20

estimates for 1973 will change over the period 1973-2000. The changes in

ence of fertility decline in Korea and Singapore. The derived age specific
fertility rates are given below and the pattern is depicted in Chart 9096.

1).       The projected expectation of life at birth indicates an increase

48 years to 62 years during the same period. 1/
Required Effort for Achieving the Objectives
6.        A simple method of calculation is adopted to estimate the number
of births that needs to be averted to achieve the plan objectives and the
required number of acceptors of various methods (Appendix I). We are fully
conscious of the limitations of the methodology adopted as well as of the
assumptions regarding continuation rates, composition of methods and age
composition of acceptors. Yet the present exercise has its usefulness in
indicating the broad magnitude of efforts required and possible achieve-
ments through the present facilities and programs. The number of births
to be averted during the first and the second plan periods and by the end
of the century are given below:
1/   a.   It is debatable if the female expectation of life is higher than
that of males at present. The life tables for 1956 for West
Bengal shows this pattern as well as the Hashims estimates for
Bangladesh for 1962.
b.   The expectation of life at birth for both sexes is expected to
increase by 0.6 years per annum between 1973-79, 0.7 years between
1979-93 and 0.5 years between 1993-2003.
c.   The computer model used for the projection employed the Coales
and Demeny Model Life Tables. For Bangladesh, Model West is
used.

7.        To achieve the long-term objective of decline in fertility, over
65 million births will need to be averted by the end of the century. For
this purpose roughly over 60Z of the women in the reproductive ages must be
motivated as family planning acceptors. This will require a gigantic ef-
fort on the part of the Government as the percentage of women practicing
family planning is very small at present. The capacity of the program to
achieve the desired results is examined in the next section.
Feasible Achievements by the Program
8.        The number of family planning acceptors that the program (includ-
ing the project) can achieve by the end of the century is based on two dif-
ferent assumptions: (a) the minimum number of acceptors that can be re-
cruited through the program without substantial improvements in the use of
resources through administration improvements by the end of the century,
and (b) the maximum number that can be achieved by the program with existing
resources if administration and operations improve substantially. The
number of acceptors is estimated under the maximum assumption on the basis
of reasonable targets that can be achieved through various program facilities
such as health and family planning services, and information and education
programs with continuous expected improvement in administration and organi-
zation. The minimum targets assume that performance at the existing level
of efficiency would be continuous. (See Tables 2 and 3 for the underlying
targets.)
Maximum Assumptions
9.   a.   The number of thana health complexes (with three subcenters)
would increase from 160 in 1973 to 356 by 1978. The existing
100 MCH centers will continue to function. The number of new
acceptors per facility (including the FFWWs) will increase
from 70 in 1975 to more than 800 in 1998.



Page 4
b.   The number of new acceptors from the model clinics is
expected to increase from 3,000 per year in 1983 to
6,000 new acceptors per year in 1998.
c.   All pilot schemes started in 61 thanas in the project
will continue in the program until 1998. The average
number of acceptors recruited will increase two to three
times by the end of the century from the level obtained
in the project period.
Minimum Assumptions
10.  a.   The thana health complex establishment would be the
same as under the maximum assumptions, but the number
of new acceptors would increase from about 50 in 1975
to 300 in 1983 (including FFWWs) and would remain at
that level until 1998.
b.   The number of new acceptors from model clinics would
remain at 3,000 from 1983 to 1998.
c.   The pilot scheme coverage will be the same as in the
maximum assumption but the number of new acceptors
remain constant.
11.       It is estimated that, at best, the program with existing I .sources
can recruit a sufficient number of acceptors that would result in a'-arting
about 23 million births by the end of the century. This would be only one-
third of the required number of 65 million births that needs to be zverted
by the end of the century to achieve a net reproduction rate of 1. If the
program does not improve, the number of births averted would be only 7 mil-
lion or 11% of the required number. It is obvious that the program Adthout
substantial improvement in organization and administration and addititonal
resources will not be able to achieve the desired reduction in fertiLity
in either the short or the long run. Even during the Five-Year PlaL period
(1973-78), the program can at best avert only one-fifth to one-third of the
required number of births (less than 1 million) to be averted. The proj-
ect's contribution is estimated on a pro rata basis according to the provi-
sion of resources by the project for different program components, and the
contribution of these components in achieving the program targets. The
project in all would avert about 1.3 million births by the end of t1;! cen-
tury. Of course, because of its short life the maximum contributior of the
project would be reached in 1978 when it will help in averting 45-5''. of
the total births that the program would avert. The data are given in the
following table:

Ways of Achieving Desired Reduction in Fertility
12.       As discussed above, the maximum feasible achievement of the exist-
ing program would be to contribute only one-third of the desired reduction
in fertility. These estimates assume the highest possible targets of re-
cruiting acceptors from the thana health complexes and from the 61 thanas
covered by the pilot schemes. The only way to achieve the remaining acceptors
needed to reach the fertility reduction objective would be to extend the
demand-creating activities on the base of the project to the rest of the
thanas and further extend the health and family planning facilities. The
extension of demand-creating activities integrated with development programs
are likely to have cumulative effects. Similar would be the position with
respect to further extension of family planning services that would be a
part of maternal and child health services. The latter would help in the
reduction of infant mortality and, therefore, in further stimulating the
attitude in favor of lower fertility. However, central to the real success
of the program in addition to the right strategy is the efficient organiza-
tion and administration needed to implement it, coupled with a strong poli-
tical commitment.

A.NNEX 2 6
A'ppendix I
PaLe l
BANGLADESH
POPULATION PROJECT
Methodology
1.        This appendix summarizes the method of calculating the births
to be averted and the required number of acceptors. It also gives the
assumptions used for such estimates.
Methodology
2.        The required number of births to be averted is taken as the
difference between births with constant fertility projections and births
with an assumed decline in fertility. The births averted are then multi-
plied by the reciprocal general fertility rate to arrive at the required
couple years of protection. Continuation rates of different methods derived
from the data available from similar countries are then applied to estimate
the number of acceptors.
Assumptions
3.        Contraceptive Mix:  The contraceptive mix, i.e., percentage of
acceptors by the use of methods, is assumed to change over the period 1974-
2000 as follows:
1974-84    1985-94    1995-2000
IUD                          15           30         35
Sterilization                  5          10         25
Orals                        40           30         20
Condoms                      40           30         20
The above assumptions are based on:
a.   The observation of recent trends indicating that
contraceptive use (1973-74) has shifted towards
oral contraceptives and is likely to remain so as long
as the family welfare worker makes the supply of
pills available.
b.   The establishment of commercial distribution of
condoms, as proposed by the Government, would
help in increasing their use.



A"N:EX 26
Appendix I
Page 2
c.   The inadequate availability of clinic facilities
at present in rural areas would limit the use of
IUDs and sterilization but would increase the use
later when the rural health infrastructure has
expanded.
4.        Continuation Rates:  The continuation rates for different methods
are assumed to improve over a period of time. The assumed continuation rates
for different methods are based on data available from different programs
at different levels of efficiency (Table 4).
Age Structure of Acceptors
5.        The age composition of users of individual methods generally differ.
On the basis of available data the assumed age composition of acceptors is
given in Table 5. The personal preference for different contraceptives at
various ages is assumed not to change. Hence, the age composition of acceptors
is held constant.
